Inching toward an apartheid state
The Israeli regime, which showed tolerance, conditional on good behavior, toward the Palestinian public in Israel and its leaders, is beginning to move in the direction of dark regimes. The apartheid regime of South Africa is an example of the kind of regime to which Israel may deteriorate.
By Assad Ganem	Jan. 1, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
The assault on the political activity of Palestinians in Israel over the past few days is unprecedented, even compared to the assailment on the Al-Arad movement in the 1960s. Most of the Palestinian public in Israel and all of its prominent leaders express a position in favor of acknowledgment of the State of Israel; but, at the same time, oppose the discriminatory regime that the Jews have established over the last 50 years.

The Israeli regime has firmly established its position as one that first and foremost serves the Jews in Israel and the Diaspora; and in order to do so, it is willing to take harsh measures against the Palestinian public in Israel. Land expropriation, citizenship laws, immigration laws, orientation of capital, and the development and division of the geographic space are all basic components of the structure of the Jewish-Israel regime. All these means constitute tools to strengthen the Jewish majority and exclude the Palestinian minority. In many cases, they even serve as tools to harm the minority.

The extension of the right to political participation to the Palestinian minority that remained in Israel after the deportation of the remainder of the Palestinians was a step that expressed two intentions toward the minority with the aim of serving the majority. The intention was, on the one hand, to include the minority in response to demands from the international community and also reflected the position of some of the founding fathers of the state who saw Israel as a Western and democratic state. But, on the other hand, it also served to include the Palestinians in the political process as a means of control aimed at creating a division between them and the other Palestinians and creating a false hope with regard to their integration into the society emerging in Israel.

The disqualification of Ahmed Tibi's candidacy by the Central Elections Committee and the expected hearing on the petition submitted by the attorney general to disqualify the Balad list come on top of steps, apparently coordinated, that have been taken - or will be taken in the near future - against other factions among the Palestinian public in Israel. Among these steps, one can cite the closure of the Islamic Movement's newspaper, attempts to undermine some of the leaders of the Islamic Movement and, in particular, the desire to deal a mortal blow to the emerging new movement of election boycotters and seekers of institutional alternatives outside of the Jewish-Israeli power base.

These attempts, which have intensified in recent weeks, are proof of the negative shift in the foundations of the Jewish-Israeli regime toward becoming a regime that acts against some of its citizens, especially in view of the fact that in the last decade nothing significant has changed in the views and positions of the various political factions among the Palestinians in Israel.

The Israeli regime, which showed tolerance, conditional on good behavior, toward the Palestinian public in Israel and its leaders, is beginning to move in the direction of dark regimes. The apartheid regime of South Africa, until the revolution of the end of the previous century, is an example of the kind of regime to which Israel will deteriorate if it continues to adopt an extreme right-wing line against its Palestinian citizens, even if whoever leads the way, or justifies it, is a representative of the law, such as the attorney general or even the High Court of Justice.

The challenge currently facing Jews and Palestinians who wish to live together is how to deter the attempts of the nationalist Israeli right to make all our lives insufferable, and how to offer an alternative that will ensure the inclusion of Palestinian civilians - and not their exclusion. This must be done both out of a commitment to true equality based on personal and collective equality for all parts of Israeli society, including the Palestinians, and also out of a commitment to regime change and the inception of a democratic regime based on a a fair and open outlook to all citizens - and not one that systematically assails its Palestinian citizens.

This is our challenge for the future, and certainly the challenge facing the Supreme Court in the coming days.

Dr. Assad Ganem is a lecturer in political science at Haifa University and an associate director of Sikkuy: The Association for the Advancement of Equal Opportunity


Home Print Edition News
U.S. says wants 'broad participation' in Knesset elections
Boucher says administration will follow closely High Court appeal against Tibi, Bishara, Balad disqualifications.
By Haaretz Staff	and Reuters	| Jan. 1, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
The United States said late Thursday it wanted to see broad participation in this month's elections, implicitly criticizing the preliminary disqualification by the Central Elections Committee of Israeli Arab MKs Ahmed Tibi and Azmi Bishara and Bishara's Balad party.

"We're of course interested in broad participation in the political process in Israel, as we are elsewhere," State Department spokesman Richard Boucher told a daily briefing.

"I'm not commenting on the particular individuals or the parties or the political aspects of this. I'm commenting on the question of the broadest possible participation in the political process," he added.

The Supreme Court is scheduled to hear next Tuesday the disqualified Arab candidates' appeal of the election committee's decision to bar them from running in the Knesset. It will also hear an appeal against the committee's decision to allow far-right Herut candidate Baruch Marzel to run.

Eleven judges will hear the appeal of Bishara and Balad, whom the CEC disqualified early Wednesday, and MK Ahmed Tibi, who was disqualified Monday. The court will have no more than two days to deliberate as it must come to a conclusion by January 9.

If Balad and Bishara are not reinstated, Balad is likely to call on Israeli Arabs to boycott the elections.

"Azmi and Balad are one and the same," said Jamal Zahalka, No. 2 on the Balad list. "Our approach is that even if only Bishara's disqualification is upheld, the entire Balad list won't compete in the elections."

Balad plans to conduct a protest gathering at the weekend in Nazareth, and demonstrations are expected Wednesday in Tel Aviv and Taibeh, where Tibi lives, to protest his disqualification.

"This is a totally political decision," said Zahalka. "The Israeli right is continuing to try to create political apartheid for Arabs in Israel."

Boucher said the United States was following closely the case of the Israeli Arab politicians. "We'll... see what happens as it proceeds to review by the courts," he added.


Home Print Edition News
CEC mulls employing external council for High Court hearings
11 judges to decide whether to uphold Central Elections Committee decision to disqualify Bishara, Balad and allow to Marzel to run.
By Haaretz Staff	| Jan. 1, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
The presidium of the Central Elections Committee met Wednesday to debate a proposal to retain the services of an outside attorney to represent it at next week's High Court hearing of appeals against the decision to disqualify MK Ahmed Tibi and to allow the candidacy of Herut number two Baruch Marzel.

Likud Knesset member Michael Eitan and Yehuda Avidan of Shas claimed at the meeting that, since the attorney general's representative on the CEC, attorney Talia Sasson, had expressed a different opinion to that of the committee itself, the CEC must be represented by an external attorney. They claimed that this is to counter any possible accusations of improper decision-making, such as unprofessional behavior and being unprepared for the hearings.

The chairman of the CEC, Justice Mishael Cheshin, did not state his view on the issue, but told the presidium that he would accept its decision. The Labor Party has petitioned the High Court over the decision to allow Marzel to compete. In their petition, which has been signed by 17 members of the CEC, the plaintiffs claim that the decision is unreasonable and fundamentally flawed, both legally and in terms of the facts.

They claim that the committee ignored the evidence presented, the relevant legal instructions and legal precedents, as well as the opinion of Cheshin himself. Labor sources added that the decision to allow Marzel to run endangers the foundations of the rule of law, by severely damaging the principle that Israel is a democratic country. They added that the ruling allows Marzel and his associates to abuse the democratic process to enter the Knesset and to spread racist and extremist views therein. According to Labor, Marzel is a Trojan horse for the banned Kach movement he once headed.

Katsav: Banning candidates no cause for crisis in Arab sector President Moshe Katsav said Wednesday that in his opinion the CEC decision to disqualify Arab candidates did not justify a break of trust between Arabs and Jews in Israel.

"The Central Elections Committee decision does not decrease the representation of Israeli Arabs in the Knesset, only the Arab voters, with their vote alone, determine the number of Arab representatives in the next Knesset," Katsav said.

Katsav also said that final decision to disqualify Knesset candidate lists will be made by the Supreme Court and not by the (CEC), which disqualified MK Azmi Biashara, his Balad party and MK Ahmed Tibi from running in the Knesset elections.

The Supreme Court is scheduled to hear next Tuesday the disqualified Arab candidates' appeal of the election committee's decisions to bar them from running in the Knesset. It will also hear an appeal against the committee's decision to allow far-right Herut candidate Baruch Marzel to run.

Eleven judges will hear the appeal of MK Azmi Bishara and his Balad party, whom the CEC disqualified early Wednesday, and MK Ahmed Tibi, who was disqualified Monday. The court will have no more than two days to deliberate because it must come to a conclusion by January 9.

If Balad and Bishara are not reinstated, Balad is likely to call on Israeli Arabs to boycott the elections.

"Azmi and Balad are one and the same," said Jamal Zahalka, No. 2 on the Balad list. "Our approach is that even if only Bishara's disqualification is upheld, the entire Balad list won't compete in the elections."

Balad plans to conduct a protest gathering at the end of the week in Nazareth, and demonstrations are expected Wednesday in Tel Aviv and Taibeh, where Tibi lives, to protest his disqualification.

"This is a totally political decision," said Zahalka. "The Israeli right is continuing to try to create political apartheid for Arabs in Israel."

Former foreign minister Shimon Peres (Labor) said Wednesday that disqualifying Tibi, Bishara and Balad is a mistake and will increase the division between Jews and Arabs.

The election committee's Wednesday decision overrode the opinion of committee chairman High Court Justice Mishael Cheshin, who said Bishara and Balad should be allowed to run, but was supported by Attorney General Elyakim Rubinstein.

The decision, taken by a one-vote margin, followed controversial decisions earlier this week to disqualify the Knesset candidacy of Arab MK Ahmed Tibi and allow extreme rightist Baruch Marzel to run.

Labor Party Secretary Ophir Pines-Paz and senior Labor lawman MK Effi Oshaya said Wednesday they plan to appeal the committee's decision to allow Marzel to run. Justice Minister Meir Sheetrit refused their request to join the petition, Army Radio reported.

Cheshin was quoted as telling the committee that Bishara's past expressions of support of the militant pro-Iranian Hezbollah in Lebanon had angered him, but that "Israel's democracy is strong and can tolerate irregular cases." He said there was insufficient evidence to disqualify Bishara or Balad.

In contrast, Rubinstein had charged that Bishara and Balad reject Israel's existence as the state of the Jewish people and support the armed struggle against Israel. By law, either of those charges is sufficient to disqualify either a party or an individual.

The decision to disqualify Tibi went against the opinions of both Cheshin and Rubinstein.

On Wednesday, Bishara tried to address all the points raised by Rubinstein, one by one.

"I believe that a people living under occupation has the right to fight against it, but I never called on the Palestinians to embark on an armed struggle against Israel. I never supported violent activity," he said.

Cheshin and several committee members tried to elicit a clear denunciation from Bishara of attacks on either civilians, soldiers or settlers, in Israel or in the territories. Bishara's response was: "Attacks on innocent civilians, wherever they are, are unacceptable to me."

Asked why he has never publicly denounced attacks on women and children, he responded that no law obliges him to do so, but on the personal level, he is appalled by the murder of children, or by incidents such as the lynching of two reservists in Ramallah at the start of the intifada.

Bishara attacked Rubinstein not only for requesting his disqualification, but also for supporting his request with material about Bishara collected by the Shin Bet security service. This material, he charged, contained "blatant lies" - such as that he had urged Balad supporters to throw stones at demonstrations or that he favors expelling all Jews who immigrated after 1948.


Home Print Edition News
CEC weighing use of external counsel for High Court hearings
11 judges to decide whether to uphold Central Elections Committee decision to disqualify Bishara, Balad and allow to Marzel to run.
By Haaretz Staff	| Jan. 1, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit

Home Print Edition Opinion
Letters to the Editor
Jan. 2, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Where are we going?

Israel's first president, Chaim Weizmann, said in 1947 that in the Jewish state, "there must not be one law for the Jews and another for the Arabs." The decision to disqualify MK Azmi Bishara and his party, Balad, from running in the Knesset elections, taken by the political appointees in the Central Elections Committee against the views of its chair, Supreme Court Justice Mishael Cheshin, is exactly what Weizmann feared.

Former Supreme Court justice Yitzhak Zamir said the committee's behavior was "scandalous and shameful;" while Cheshin likened the committee to a circus and a kindergarten. How then can its decision annul the promise in Israel's Declaration of Independence that the state "will ensure complete equality of social and political rights to all its inhabitants, irrespective of religion, race or sex?"

MK Bishara called much of the Shin Bet security service inspired "evidence" against him - that he denied the existence of Israel as a Jewish state and that he supported an armed struggle against the state - "blatant lies." He said as an Israeli citizen who supported the national struggle of the Palestinian people, he was not part of the Israeli consensus; but now they also wanted to deny his right to be part of the controversy.

Defending Bishara's rightto run for the Knesset must be seen by all Israelis who cherish democracy - be they Zionist or post-Zionist, secular or religious, left or right - as a dividing line between democracy and equal rights, on the one side, and legalized discrimination and apartheid, on the other.

Now, the Supreme Court must make a fateful decision that will resolve in which direction Israeli society is moving.

Dan Leon

Jerusalem


Home Print Edition News
Argentine immigrants recruited to `stop Arab takeover' in Katzir
By Mazal Mualem	| Jan. 5, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Five new-immigrant families from Argentina arrived at the Harish community in Wadi Ara last week, joining the six immigrant families who arrived at the beginning of last month as part of the Katzir-Harish local council's effort "to stop the creeping Arab settlement" there.

Thirty more Argentinian families, who were persuaded to come to Israel specifically for this purpose, are to arrive in Harish in the next few weeks.

Katzir-Harish council head Dubi Sanderov said that since the beginning of the Al-Aqsa Intifada, Arab organizations, including the Higher Arab Monitoring Committee, have been acting to settle Arabs in Katzir and Harish for "political and provocative reasons" and "it is possible that Arab elements outside Israel are encouraging it."

"We have no problem with individual Arabs interested in living here, but we feel there is an organized takeover bid, and we have no intention of giving in to it. We're acting to bring new immigrants here, because this is one way of preserving the Jewish character of these communities," Sanderov said.

The Katzir and Harish communities were established at the beginning of the 1980s as part of then housing minister Ariel Sharon's "seven star" plan to beef up Jewish settlement in the north. Despite Sharon's vision of settling 30,000 Jews in the two communities, their population currently totals only 3,500, 40 percent of whom are new immigrants, and 150 Arabs (70 of whom are members of the Karaja clan who were moved there from Ramle).

But the council's attractive offers and relatively low housing costs failed to attract established residents, especially since the intifada began. In addition, dozens of families have abandoned the communities following the outbreak of the Al-Aqsa Intifada and put their homes up for rent at especially low prices - $200 to $250 for a four-room apartment. Now, in anticipation of the war in Iraq, many are keeping their homes empty so they can flee to them in emergency, assuming the area is protected since it lies in the heart of Arab territory.

In this situation, new immigrants have become much in demand. When it appeared that many Argentinian Jews are in severe financial straits due to the economic crisis there, the council sent Pichi Dubiner, who arrived 20 years ago from Argentina, on a recruiting mission. Dubiner went to Argentina and managed to persuade 40 families, most of them young couples with one to three children, to immigrate to Katzir and Harish.

"I explained to them that the whole state of Israel is under the same security threat, and that they would be coming to a rural community with a high quality of life," he said.

Harish prepared a warm, cordial welcome for the new immigrants, giving each family a furnished apartment and an adoptive family.

Shawki Khatib, head of the Higher Arab Monitoring Committee called Sanderov's claims of an attempted Arab takeover of Katzir and Harish "paranoia."

Committee spokesman Abed Anabtawi labeled Sanderov's claims "unfounded nonsense. We are indeed acting to enable every Arab to live wherever he wants to, as part of his legitimate rights as a citizen, otherwise this would be an apartheid state." He denied there was any organized bid to settle Arabs in a specific place.

In March 2000 the High Court of Justice forbade the State of Israel to discriminate between Arabs and Jews in land allocation. The precedent-setting ruling came in response to the petition of the couple Adal and Iman Qadan, whose attempt to buy a home in Katzir, a community established by the Jewish Agency, was rejected. The High Court ruled in their favor.

But despite the ruling, Arabs who wish to build a home in Katzir are rejected by the community's acceptance committee, which must approve anyone wishing to buy land. However, renting or buying a Housing Ministry-built apartment does not require the committee's approval and there are 35 such apartments available in Katzir and 140 in Harish.

The council is making no secret of trying to prevent Arabs from inhabiting these apartments. One way of doing so is through the municipal debt collection department, which reports every case in which Arabs try to find out whether the previous tenants of a particular apartment have debts. This usually indicates an interest in buying or renting an apartment, and the council then tries to take over the property and place new immigrants in it.


Home Article Print Page
Letters to the Editor
Jan. 6, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Fears of disloyalty

Those Jews among us who grew up in the Diaspora are well acquainted with the common accusation: you Jews have dual loyalties and cannot be trusted to be loyal to the state. We did our best to prove that we really were loyal and did not put "international Jewish concerns" and Israel before our responsibilities as citizens. But our concern for the well-being of our fellow Jews never left our minds, and we continued our Zionist activities.

Since 1948 and the establishment of Israel, the Palestinian Israelis have been suspected of being a fifth column. However, until the second intifada, more Jews than Muslims or Christians were arrested and convicted of spying against the state. Yet, our Jewish citizenry is still so insecure as to accuse every Palestinian Israeli politician of disloyalty. Yes, they are concerned about their fellow Palestinians who are being starved and oppressed by Israel. Yes, they try to help them. Yes, they are looking for a peaceful solution to the problem.

While the Jewish idea of a suitable solution might not be identical with that of the Palestinian Israelis, this does not make them traitors. There can never be a solution that is not acceptable to the Palestinians themselves, whether Israeli or otherwise. Peace is achieved through mutual efforts, a fact that seems to escape the consciousness of many Israelis. Punishing Palestinian Israeli politicians because of our fears of their disloyalty is counter-productive. What would have happened if the United States had treated American Jews the way we are treating non-Jews here? What would we have said and thought?

The time has come to stop justifying our biased treatment of the stranger in our midst on the basis of our fears. We are just forcing them to be disloyal in order to protect themselves and their brothers over the border.

Deborah Nothmann

Haifa

A higher good

I am writing in support of those members of the Israel Defense Forces who refuse to serve in the occupied territories. The recent Supreme Court decision rejecting their claim of "selective" conscientious objection is just plain wrong in its logic and reasoning. These individuals, while perhaps not heroes to the majority of Israelis, are upholding the value of moral conscience. Their refusal to honor a military directive on grounds of adhering to a higher moral good is a hallowed strategy in all civil liberties struggles.

The best analogy to recent American history I can think of is the civil rights movement. Martin Luther King held that laws which denied basic rights to blacks could and should be violated because the "law breaking"

protesters were following a "higher law." His white opponents, like Israel's Supreme Court in its ruling, said that King's views would lead to social chaos because every citizen would pick and choose which laws they wished to obey and which they chose to disobey. But King's position has withstood the test of time. American society did not collapse in a sea of lawlessness. Rather, the majority of Americans eventually came to see King's struggle as a righteous one; and they rejected the Southern white conservatives who warned of social chaos.

Richard Silverstein

Seattle, WA

Voice of the Other

Regarding "Five rules set by the Kingdom of the Settlements" by Amira Hass and "Inching toward an apartheid state" by Assad Ganem, January 1:

In their articles both Dr. Assad Ganem and Amira Hass provided an accurate analysis of Israeli society. Unfortunately, the situation that they have described is a chilling one of ethnocratic rule on the part of Jewish Israelis who find "uppity" Arab MKs, who've accurately described both the discrimination faced by their constituents and the repression of the occupation, guilty of the crime of political expression that somehow contradicts the normative (ultra) Zionist paradigm. Their sentence, of course, is to be silenced. Yet, the silence imposed upon them is deafening. It screams out that we are unwilling to allow the voice of the Other to be heard. This is most frightening and disturbing.

Louis Frankenthaler

Jerusalem


Home Print Edition Sports
British minister calls for Zimbabwe boycott
By Reuters	| Jan. 6, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
LONDON - British government minister Peter Hain has called for the England cricket team to boycott their World Cup match in Zimbabwe, saying to play there would be a "propaganda victory" for the country's president Robert Mugabe.

"I don't think England cricketers should go - and if they did and if the World Cup goes ahead in Harare and Bulawayo, it will be exploited and milked mercilessly by Robert Mugabe and his odious regime whilst people starve outside," Welsh Secretary Hain said on BBC television yesterday.

Zimbabwe is scheduled to host six of the 54 matches in the World Cup tournament, which is being staged mainly in South Africa from February 9 to March 23. England is due to play Zimbabwe in Harare on February 13.

Mugabe has received widespread criticism for his controversial land reform program, a policy opponents blame for the country's deepening economic crisis and food shortages.

The British government has said it cannot order the England team not to play in Zimbabwe, while the England and Wales Cricket Board has said it wants a decision from the government. The two sides are set to meet later this week.

Hain, who as a young man campaigned against the apartheid regime in South Africa, said: "We're not telling the cricket authorities to do anything. We are saying it's morally wrong to play and give Mugabe and his tyranny a propaganda victory, which is what will happen.

"The idea that cricketers or cricket officials are absolved from moral decisions seems to me to be wrong. We all have to take moral decisions in the jobs that we do."

He added: "We don't think we should be handing Mugabe a propaganda victory anymore than we should have handed Hitler in 1936 a propaganda victory that he got by staging the Berlin Olympics as he did then."


Home News
Israel preventing PA parliament speaker from attending session
By The Associated Press	| Jan. 13, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Palestinian parliament speaker Ahmed Qureia (Abu Ala) said Monday that Israel had prevented him from traveling to Ramallah to open a session of the legislature. The army, which oversees travel through military checkpoints in the West Bank and Gaza Strip, said it was checking the report.

The Cabinet decided last week to limit travel by Palestinian officials as part of its response to suicide bombings in Tel Aviv that killed 23 people.

Qureia said he requested authorization from Israeli officials to cross through two military checkpoints between his hometown of Abu Dis on the outskirts of Jerusalem and Ramallah so that he could attend a session of the Palestinian Legislative Council. He said he was not given permission to make the trip.

"This is a continuation of the Israeli policy to implement the apartheid system and isolate the Palestinian people from each other," Qureia said in a telephone interview. "This is also an indicator of the Israeli intention to paralyze the Palestinian Authority and destroy it completely."

Last week, the Israeli Cabinet also decided to bar several senior Palestinian officials from attending an international conference in London. They were to discuss reforming the Palestinian Authority, one of several steps demanded by the U.S. administration and other governments as a precursor to the formation of a Palestinian state.


Home Print Edition Opinion
The infants tell the real story
Those looking at the Middle East from afar can see the gaps between the Jews in their country and the Muslims in their countries growing, and continuing to grow, while the gaps inside the besieged island shrink and continue to shrink, despite lengthy conflict and hostility.
By Amnon Rubinstein	Jan. 15, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
The infant mortality rate in British Mandate Palestine in 1943-44, the last year for which such data are available, was almost 10 percent for Muslims, 3.5 percent for Jews, and 7 percent for Christians. There were three times as many infant deaths among Muslims, and twice as many among Christians, as there were among Jews.

Mortality rates for children up to the age of 5 were even more disgraceful - 21 percent among Muslims, 4.5 percent among Jews, and 10 percent among Christians. Muslims suffered a rate five times more, and Christians twice as much, as Jews. (The data are from the British Mandate Authority's annual statistical yearbook in the National Archives).

In Israel 2001 all the mortality rates were dramatically lower of course, but no less important, gaps between Jews and Arabs have also shrunk. The infant mortality rate among Jews was 0.14 percent, among Muslims 0.82 percent, and among Christians 0.2 percent. The gap between Jews and Muslims had dropped by a third and the gap between Jews and Christians was reversed.

Even more dramatic was the narrowing of the gap in child mortality rates among children up to the age of 5. The Muslim rate sank from the 21 percent to 0.5 percent and the gap between Muslims to Jews had shrunk from five times more to double.

This is dramatic, unprecedented progress unknown to the public, and is in contrast with Arab states where infant mortality is much higher than in Israel among all population groups. Infant mortality in wealthy Kuwait, for example, is 1.2 percent - twice that of Muslims in Israel.

Infant and child mortality rates are one of the unequivocal measures of success in a welfare state. Infant mortality is an indicator of the situation in the Middle East. Israel is a besieged island in an Arab and Muslim region, but the siege has only increased the enormous gaps between the Jewish state and the world around it.

Inside the besieged island live a minority that in culture, religion and nationality belong to those besieging the island, and it also suffers from a sense of discrimination and real discrimination.

But those looking at the Middle East from afar can see the gaps between the Jews in their country and the Muslims in their countries growing, and continuing to grow, while the gaps inside the besieged island shrink and continue to shrink, despite lengthy conflict and hostility.

This has enormous long-term importance, even while a gaping chasm between Jews and Arabs in Israel persists. We must not rest until all the gaps between all parts of Israeli society have been eliminated in general, and between Jews and Arabs in particular.

But at a time when Israel is criticized at home and abroad as an apartheid state, it is worth noting the infant mortality rates and the shrinking gaps in education and health from the past to the present, as an indication of reality.

It is a complex reality and very different from the mendacious, fashionable statements made about Israel. A paradox has been created in this reality. In the years that the political polarization between Jews and Arabs in Israel, especially of course, because of the war with the Palestinians, the social polarization has narrowed, at least as far as it is measured in health and education data. Even the most vehement critic of Israel cannot allow himself to ignore this data.

Letters to the Editor
Jan. 15, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Sharon is a life- long intimidator

Regarding "Humiliation undermines security," by Alouph Hareven, Ha'aretz January 12 Alouph Hareven rightly calls for training soldiers to treat average Palestinians with dignity, but he dances around the invisible elephant in the room. Such a change will never happen without the prime minister setting a tone of respect from the top. And the current prime minister will never do it.

Public statements and gestures offer tremendous potential. Based on good will, they can nudge the emotional climate in a better direction, something that is desperately needed. Prime Minister Ariel Sharon's words and deeds, however, reveal him as a lifelong provoker and intimidator, not a trust-builder.

An Israeli prime minister today should be working tirelessly to identify, encourage and strengthen coexistence-minded Palestinian moderates and respond positively to their gestures. The old Sharon strategy of heavier retaliation can never secure the future, as Amram Mitzna points out.

Part of the formula for real peace is for the prime minister to affirm publicly, once and for all, basic respect for the human dignity that Palestinians share with all peoples as a birthright and to acknowledge their legitimate national narrative. Another part is to create many more opportunities for Jewish-Palestinian dialogue. The fact that most Israelis have never had a conversation with a Palestinian contributes greatly to the mutual demonization. Men like Anwar Sadat and Yitzhak Rabin were able to transcend the past. They had the courage to reach beyond it and express a vision of peace to their own people and even more significantly, to the other side. Amram Mitzna is clearly in that mold. Ariel Sharon never can be.

Jeffrey R. Stone

Milton, MA

One man, one vote, one country

Regarding "Like sheep to a Palestinian state," by Nadav Shragai, Ha'aretz, January 13

Nadav Shragai says about a proposed Palestinian state that "all the signs indicate that the Palestinians' motivation to see us destroyed is not waning, rather the opposite."

But if this is true, it spells the death knell of the two-state solution. Let's get real. It is also perfectly understandable. Underlying Israel was as much ethnic-religious cleansing of the Palestinians from their land as possible, and refusing their return.

What Israelis and Palestinians alike need more than ever is a nonviolent civil rights movement that profoundly concurs with the Likud's official platform that the whole of Palestine, including the West Bank and Gaza, belong to Israel. Then, in the same way as what happened in the U.S. and South Africa, the movement should call for a democratic system of one man-one vote.

The state finds itself in this position: Having already ethnically cleansed and refused the return of 750,000 Palestinians now you have had to occupy more land in order to protect the land you have already stolen; and many of you have wanted to settle and expand into and steal this land as well. To adopt the simple and fundamental democratic Western principle of one man-one vote on both sides of the Jordan in all of Eretz Israel, that is, the dismantling of the ethnic cleansing and apartheid, would change this troubled and violence-ridden society, just as the apartheid-era South Africa and the Jim Crow South and the Confederacy were changed by Tutu and Mandela, and King and the American Civil Rights Movement, and Abraham Lincoln. Lincoln's motto holds: "With malice toward none and charity for all."

James Adler

Cambridge, MA


Home Print Edition Opinion
Reflections in a Cypriot mirror
In another era, light years from the present, Israel was praised as an example of social progress and political creativity in a democracy that prospered under the most dire conditions. Yet the sharpest edge of criticism has been blunted by being cast into a familiar international mold - "the model of polarized societies."
By Meron Benvenisti	| Jan. 16, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
In another era, light years from the present, Israel was praised as an example of social progress and political creativity in a democracy that prospered under the most dire conditions. True, the endless Israeli-Arab conflict and its violent fallout have somewhat dulled the shine on the success story and there has always been considerable criticism of ethnic discrimination and of the political, economic and social inequities faced by the Arab minority.

Yet the sharpest edge of this criticism has been blunted by being cast into a familiar international mold - "the model of polarized societies" - that made Israel just one society among many with "intercommunal disputes." These include the Catholics and Protestants of Northern Ireland, Maronites and Shi'ites in Lebanon, whites and blacks in South Africa, Sinhalese and Tamils in Sri Lanka, Greeks and Turks in Cyprus, Germans and Italians in southern Tyrol - and in Israel, Jews and Arabs.

The feeling that your troubles are shared by others provides a certain relief, and comparable causes or a common denominator create distance, making it easier to cope with painful dilemmas. In the mid-1980s this "comparative research" fad reached a peak. Intellectual arguments raged about models for "ethnic control" and "ethno-national disputes," as opposed to "wars of national liberation" or "colonialism," as opposed to "pluralist cleavages," and "apartheid" as opposed to "ethnic democracy." All the arguments just diverted attention from the slippery slope.

Starting in the late 1980s and on into the mid-1990s, dramatic developments were afoot in the realm of intercommunal disputes. As if a magic wand had been waved, they grew more moderate, so much so that many people began to believe they had finally been "resolved."

This was true of Lebanon, South Africa, Northern Ireland and even in Israel-Palestine, during the euphoric "Oslo" years. In the same way that the organic, endemic insolubility of the conflict was previously emphasized, now theories were expounded about the "point of ripeness" of the conflict. With that point reached, the road to a solution was open.

Although especially violent and barbaric ethnic conflicts persisted in Bosnia, Rwanda and Sri Lanka, their tides too were beginning to ebb. Permanent solutions were not reached in any of these societies - such solutions may be impossible - but in most of them, internal or external forces succeeded in realigning the dispute toward dialogue and crisis management. There have been signs of late that even the ancient conflicts that tore apart Cyprus and Sri Lanka are getting close to settlement, and that the rival sides have set out on the road of negotiation that will lead to agreement on models of autonomy and power sharing in government.

Not only does the Israeli-Palestinian conflict show no signs of temperance, but the opposite - intensification - is obvious in both its verbal and violent expression. Indeed, in the same way that in a different era Israel was seen as an example of progress, it has now become a symbol of an extremist, uncompromising state that has forsaken every universal norm concerning minority rights.

It is no accident that Israel is now a symbol for the most extremist groups fighting against compromise in Northern Ireland and in Cyprus. An Israeli flag flies proudly along the border of the Protestant zone in Northern Belfast, as a sign of defiant challenge - no concessions to Catholics.

The "foreign minister" of the Cypriot Turks joins Israelis who condemn "Oslo" and finds support from those Israelis who "have expressed their hope that a wall will someday divide them [from Palestinians], like that which runs across the island and slices the capital Nicosia in two - no concessions to Greeks, whose sole aim is seen as "Hellenization" of Turkish Cyprus, in the guise of a "right of return."

This identification with Israelis is no coincidence, just as it is no accident that only the Israeli-Palestinian conflict is deteriorating at a time when similar conflicts are becoming more moderate. Israel remains the only country in the Western world - maybe now in the whole world - that does not hesitate to create facts that are designed to make one ethnic group, the Jews, gain full control over the geophysical, political and cultural resources of the minority Arab national group. It even sees escalation of violence - in the guise of a "war on terror" - as a way to achieve the "absolute victory" of its ethnic objectives.

It is no wonder that compared to other ethno-national groups, which opted (willingly or grudgingly) for a rational approach inevitably leading to settling the disputes, Israel's defiant and confrontational approach is conspicuous.

There was a time when one could be comforted by the fact that Israel was not alone. Now, when the Turks and Greeks, Tamils and Sinalese, are all trying to settle their ancient and bloody conflicts, all eyes are focused on the anachronism of a country that professes to belong to the enlightened world, but permits itself to behave as if nothing has happened in the past generation. If the two sides on the neighboring island can reach agreement - or even dialogue - it will be impossible to bear the shame here any longer.


Home Print Edition Features
Corridors of Power / Assessing Arafat
Last week, Major General Amos Gilad gave a talk at the Middle East Media and Research Institute (MEMRI) in Jerusalem in which he offered his take on Yasser Arafat and the Palestinian Authority leader's intentions.
By Uzi Benziman	| Jan. 17, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Last week, Major General Amos Gilad gave a talk at the Middle East Media and Research Institute (MEMRI) in Jerusalem in which he offered his take on Yasser Arafat and the Palestinian Authority leader's intentions.

While Gilad has said similar things on numerous occasions, both in public and in private, the way he delineated the situation this time made it clear that this issue ought to be at the center of the election campaign. Based on his long acquaintance with Arafat (Gilad was, among other things, the head of the research division in military intelligence and is currently Coordinator of Government Activity in the West Bank and Gaza), he asserted that it will never be possible to make peace with him.

Gilad believes that Arafat will not be satisfied with a two-state solution and that his true aim is "Greater Palestine" - a Palestinian state extending from the desert to the sea that would include Israel, the West Bank and Gaza, and even Jordan. According to Gilad, Arafat has not abandoned this vision despite his surprise at Israel's resilience in the face of the ongoing terror attacks.

Arafat was betting that the terror campaign he methodically prepared under the cover of the Oslo accords would erode Israel's bargaining ability and enable the Palestinians to dictate the terms of a final agreement. While he has been disillusioned in that regard, he still feels that he can defeat Israel by means of the Palestinians' demographic advantage, which will continue to eat away at the fabric of Israeli existence. This is why he has remained so insistent about the right of return.

Gilad says that at the Taba talks, the Palestinians were surprised by Israel's willingness to discuss, in principle, the refugees' return.

Gilad concluded by saying that while he doesn't know what kind of positions Arafat's successors will take, he knows for certain that peace cannot be achieved as long as Arafat is the Palestinian leader.

Gilad's assessment (which is shared by others in military intelligence) underlied the approach taken by the general staff in the past few years: It dictated former Chief of Staff Shaul Mofaz's stance versus the PA and is key to the outlook of the current general staff. Of course, it also had a decisive influence on the approach taken by both Ehud Barak and Ariel Sharon to the Israeli-Palestinian conflict. Barak wanted to expose Arafat's true position and, by so doing, enhance Israeli understanding of the nature of the conflict. Sharon acts as if Barak's bitter experience only reinforces his known opinion of Arafat.

Both of them, and Barak's predecessor, Benjamin Netanyahu, managed the affairs of state in the past six years with the learned and professional opinion of Amos Gilad well in mind.

If the current election campaign really hinged on the existential issue of the future of the State of Israel in light of the conflict with the Palestinians, Gilad's view would have been at the center of the political discourse.

But the competition for the voter's hearts and minds is being waged not by politicians or ideologues, but by "strategic advisers," publicists and advertisers. And the latter bunch is causing this crucial debate to get drowned out by the din of corruption scandals, genuine and otherwise.

Lior Horev, a central figure in Sharon's campaign, has gone public with a complaint about alleged police scheming, unhesitatingly situating the Likud as a party doing battle with the law enforcement authorities (some Likud officials have been quoted as saying that the state is witness to "a conspiracy of the `rule of law' gang against the Likud").

Meanwhile, Labor has been accusing Sharon of Mafioso-like behavior without providing any decisive evidence of this.

In all the cacophony emanating from this election contest, the voter can hardly think straight.

Major General Gilad's insights ought to be a basis for the debate in which the outlooks of the right and left diverge. The right's conclusion is that Arafat's vision provides full justification for the view that says maintaining control of all the territories will serve as the main guarantee of the state's continued existence.

The left's conclusion is that separation from the West Bank and Gaza Strip is the formula for ensuring Israel's future; otherwise, it will either become an apartheid state or lose its Zionist identity.

The need to choose between these two paths grows more urgent by the day, but in the ten days left before the election, Israeli voters will not be given a decent opportunity to weigh the two possibilities. The parties will continue to distract the public from the main issue with all this extraneous clamor that dulls the senses and induces voter apathy or even despair.


Home Print Edition Opinion
Compromise still exists - in the polls
Most public opinion polls, both in Israel and in the territories, show that levels of support for compromise remain stable. The Israeli public, which is now leaning toward a vote for the right, is the same public that is willing, more than in the past, to agree to a Palestinian state and the dismantling of settlements.
By Danny Rubinstein	| Jan. 27, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
From the Palestinian perspective, there is no realistic hope that the results of tomorrow's elections will lead to change. From their point of view, if there is any change, it will probably be for the worse. The chain of events is well known: As long as the intifada and violence continue, the right wing in Israel gets stronger and calls for harsher measures against the Palestinians grow louder and louder. On the Palestinian side, the picture is much the same. The tougher Israel's policy - the more assassinations, the more curfews, the more roadblocks - so the bitterness, frustration and lust for revenge grow and with them terrorist attacks.

However, alongside this vicious cycle exists another phenomenon, on both sides, which seems to be in complete contradiction to the escalation in violence. Most public opinion polls, both in Israel and in the territories, show that levels of support for compromise remain stable. The Israeli public, which is now leaning toward a vote for the right, is the same public that is willing, more than in the past, to agree to a Palestinian state and the dismantling of settlements.

Most of the Palestinian public still supports suicide bombings in Israel (although levels of support have dropped recently from 80 percent to 60 percent), but within the Palestinian public exists a willingness to recognize the state of Israel and agree to a two-state solution. When one asks pundits in the West Bank and Gaza to explain the contradiction, the answer is that support for terrorist attacks is not synonymous with support for the destruction of the state of Israel, but reflects support for the struggle against occupation. In other words, most of the Palestinian public is willing to recognize the state of Israel and live side by side with it, but supports a battle against occupation that means using terrorist attacks to hurt Israel as much as possible.

These findings beg the question, how can one translate the willingness for compromise that exists on both sides into something more concrete. Ami Ayalon, the former head of the Shin Bet, and Sari Nusseibeh, the president of Al Quds University, tried over recent months to promote the idea of popular endorsement of a peace document formulated by the two men. The idea was that if they were to manage to get hundreds of thousands of people on both sides to sign the document, it would be possible to present it to the leaders of both nations and demand that they give it their blessing.

In Israel almost no one has heard of the idea, while on the Palestinian side it has run into difficulties. Nusseibeh was given an angry welcome when he appeared last week before students at Al-Najah University in Nablus. Now, a group of Palestinian academics and intellectuals, among them Dr. Haider a-Shafi from Gaza, are trying to get several dozen non-politically active Palestinians to sign a joint document with Israelis calling for two states based on the 1967 borders.

These initiatives seem for the moment to be unlikely to succeed as circumstances on the ground are working against them. One example is the way in which the idea of separation is being implemented with a fence going up between Israel and the territories (the apartheid fence, the Palestinians call it). The fence, which does not run along the Green Line, prevents free movement for both sides and of course leaves the Jewish settlers in the territories.

The fence also means that the only Jewish presence in Hebron, Ramallah and Nablus is of Jewish fanatics guarded by battalions of soldiers and border police. The Israeli government has for several years placed obstacles in the path of Israelis such as Peace Now members who wish to demonstrate, for example, in Hebron. Thus the only Jews remaining in Hebron are Baruch Marzel and his cronies, who day after day call for the transfer of the city's Arabs.


Home Print Edition Sports
Troubled Chelsea adrift in a sea of conspiracies
The real dramas at English Premier League club Chelsea FC are far from their Stamford Bridge ground in west London.
By Yossi Melman	| Jan. 29, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
The real dramas at English Premier League club Chelsea FC are far from their Stamford Bridge ground in west London.

Those dramas in the dealings of the club take place in board rooms of companies, some with shady reputations, in tax havens and at casinos, some on land and others aboard ships.

They rear their head in criminal cases that have sent shock waves all the way from Britain to South Africa, the U.S., Egypt, Libya and even reaching Israel. Indeed, even the Mossad and Cyril Kern find their way into this story.

Chelsea, which was founded in 1905, is currently identified very strongly with its outspoken chairman, Ken Bates, who bought a controlling interest in the club in 1982 at a very attractive price - only 1 million pounds.

In 1996 Bates floated his club on the London Stock Exchange and raised its value to 200 million pounds. For a while, things were looking good, but lately it has not been so rosy at Stamford Bridge, with losses of almost 10 million pounds in 2001 and debts totaling tens of millions of pounds. Indeed, the club's very existence is in doubt as Bates and his helpers look desperately to find a buyer.

Recently it has been alleged that the debts result not from poor management; rather, they can be blamed on various alleged con artists.

A group of Chelsea supporters are fearful that the club really is on the verge of collapse and that Bates and his secret group of partners only want the stadium as a piece of real estate, since it is situated in one of the most expensive locations in the whole of Europe.

The group, which calls itself the Chelsea Action Group (CAG), claims to continue the legacy of Matthew Harding, a former club shareholder and supporter who poured millions of his own fortune into the club and who was killed in a helicopter accident several years ago. Their web site address is www.chelseaactiongroup.com

One of the key players in this story is a South African businessman named Stanley Tollman. Born in 1930, by the age of 40 Tollman was considered to be one of the biggest real estate and hotel magnates during the apartheid years.

In 1970 he built the Johannesburg Towers Hotel, one of the most exclusive hotels in South Africa, but five years later he was broke. He moved to Europe and took ownership of a commercial and residential skyscraper complex in Monte Carlo where he met Bates.

The two became business partners and soon Tollman set up a small travel agency in London - Trafalgar Travel - for Bates.

After Bates bought Chelsea in 1982, he invited Tollman onto the club's board and Trafalgar Travel's executives were regular visitors in the directors' box at Stamford Bridge.

In 1984 Tollman and a partner named Monty Hundley bought a chain of low-price motels called Days Inn in the U.S. The chain cost $765 million and was financed mainly through bank loans.

Tollman supposedly left the Chelsea board in 1992 but lately he appears to have reappeared in a crucial role. The long-standing friendship between Tollman and Bates is still strong (Bates even called his dog Tollie).

In 1993 Bates and Tollman set up a company called Chelsea Village which itself established many subsidiaries in various tax havens around the world. Those companies moved money around among themselves.

The Chelsea Village company, which also owns the soccer club, continued to operate even after the club's shares were floated in London and suspicions are now beginning to fly that after the flotation, anonymous parties took all the money and effectively left the club penniless.

One of Bates' and Tollman's partners in the Chelsea Village company is a man called Marwan Ashraf, 58, who is married to Mona, the third daughter of former Egyptian leader Gamal Abdel Nasser. Ashraf held a senior position in Nasser's regime and he was also close to Anwar Sadat.

Ha'aretz recently revealed that according to foreign publications, Marwan Ashraf has been a senior Mossad agent since 1969 and he provided his operators early warning of the Yom Kippur War.

Eli Zeira, who was then head of IDF Military Intelligence, and who was found by the Agranat Commission to be one of those most responsible for not knowing that the war was going to break out, claimed that Ashraf was a double agent and he tricked his Israeli operators.

Ashraf left for London and went into business. Today he divides his time between London and Cairo and among other things, he guides Western entrepreneurs to invest their money in Arab countries and directs Arab funds for investment in the West.

Media speculation has also tied him to Libyan leader Muammar Gadhafi, claiming that he serves as his financial adviser.

The CAG claims that Gadhafi is a partner of Ashraf's, who himself has a 3-percent stake in Chelsea.

The Gadhafi family, who are well-known soccer fans, have already shown an interest in Italian soccer and have connections with giant Juventus. Gadhafi's son is active in the Libyan FA and is known to have little patience with unsuccessful coaches.

Meanwhile Tollman has also taken a controlling interest in a company named Alpha which runs floating casinos. These ships are a way to avoid local gambling laws along the Mississippi River and on Indian reserves in Florida.

Tollman and his wife, Beatrice, have accumulated an enormous personal fortune which includes a farm in Connecticut, an exclusive luxury apartment overlooking Central Park and a palace in Palm Beach.

Beatrice runs their five hotels in the UK and the couple, who own a fleet of Rolls Royce cars and private aircraft, soon found themselves living it up with the Palm Beach "jet set."

The Tollmans were considered to be the hottest property at Christmas and New Year's parties in Palm Beach. Tollman is also known to be a major contributor to the Republican Party.

The high life did not last for long, however, and in the late 1990s the U.S. authorities opened a criminal investigation on suspicion that the purchase of the Days Inn chain was done by defrauding the banks.

In April 2002 Tollman was convicted on 33 counts of embezzlement, fraud and tax evasion. The trial revealed that Tollman extracted $42 million by fraudulent means from U.S. banks. When he declared himself to be in financial difficulties, Tollman was in fact transferring the money to shell companies in Europe.

Tollman did not turn up for the trial and took refuge in the UK while the U.S. authorities apply for an extradition order.

So how is all this connected to Israel?

Two weeks ago Ha'aretz broke the story of Cyril Kern's $1.5 million loan to the Sharon family. The South African media began looking into Kern's dealings, and the Mail and Guardian newspaper revealed a connection between Kern and Tollman which Kern admitted. He said that a relative had introduced him to Tollman.

"I have met him, but he is not a friend and I have no business or personal connection with him," Kern said.

Kern refused to answer questions posed by Ha'aretz about the matter and said he would not even be prepared to listen to questions about the man.


Home Print Edition News
New fence plan irks left, right
By Nadav Shragai	| Feb. 5, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
There were harsh reactions from both left and right to the map presented to Defense Minister Shaul Mofaz this week by the Yesha Council of Settlements as an alternative to the separation fence route already partially approved by the government.

Peace Now called on the government to reject the proposed route, saying "the proposal to annex hundreds of thousands of Palestinians will turn Israel into an apartheid state. The proposed fence has nothing to do with security considerations and its purpose is to perpetuate the conflict and annex the West Bank." Peace Now called for the separation fence to be placed on the Green Line.

The Yesha Council called it a "security fence" and not a "political fence." It in effect annexes at least another 110,000 Palestinians by putting them on the western side of putative fence.


Home Life Arts & Leisure
Negative flashback
Confronting less pleasant aspects of our past is something we have to do, says print gallery owner and archivist Vivienne Silver-Brody. And that, she says, can be done through photography.
By David Rapp	Feb. 7, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
In the annals of the Zionist movement, it was journalist Theodor Herzl who first understood the importance of the photo-op. When the German emperor, Kaiser Wilhelm II, planned a trip to the Holy Land in the late 19th century to attend the dedication of the new Church of the Redeemer in Jerusalem's Old City, Herzl sent him an album of photographs of the nascent Jewish community in Palestine. Along with the album, he sent a letter seeking an audience with the emperor. On October 29, 1898, they met at Mikveh Israel, and the photograph of the encounter was published all over the world.

Those who want to believe that the Zionist movement was born in deceit can cite this photograph. David Wolffson, Herzl's assistant, who had taken the snap, discovered, on developing the film, that the picture had not come out. With Herzl's knowledge, a photomontage was made, reconstructing the historic event.

In the decades that followed, photography played an important role in the Zionist propaganda effort. Pictures were taken documenting immigration and the founding of settlements, building up the myth of the Jew returning to his homeland and working the soil. Alongside the commissioned photographers, there were also ordinary photographers in Palestine who were not working for major organizations like the Jewish National Fund and the Jewish Agency.

One of the photographers in Tel Aviv in the 1920s was Arnold Zylberschlag, the proprietor of Photo Roshem on Rothschild Street. The Zylberschlags arrived from Poland in 1924. Arnold also opened a factory that manufactured buckets, but was forced to close down, unable to compete with imports from Britain. The family returned to Europe in 1927.

Three generations

In those years, the Goldfarb family owned a photography studio in Kielce, Poland called Fotografia Art Rembrandt. A son of the Zylberschlags and a daughter of the Goldfarbs met at the studio in Poland and married in 1932. Eventually, the couple settled in Rhodesia (today Zimbabwe). A daughter, named Vivienne, was born to them in 1942 - the third generation of a family of photographers on both sides. Vivienne received her first camera at the age of five, and has been snapping pictures ever since.

Vivienne Silver-Brody, who immigrated to Israel in 1971, established a photographic print gallery which has been open to the public for 17 years, first in Jerusalem, and now in Ein Hod. On the door of the gallery in Ein Hod hang photographs of her family's photography shops in Tel Aviv and Poland. Over the years, Silver-Brody has made efforts to trace the history of her family, which lost many members in the Holocaust.

After moving to Israel, she began to search for her roots in a broader sense, which could explain her attraction to the history of Jewish photography in Eretz Israel. In the Jewish artists' village Ein Hod, built on the ruins of the abandoned Arab village of Ein Houd, she has spent years researching a history that she says Israeli society and the local art world have done little to preserve.

The Silver Print gallery is now showing photographs of women and children taken from the 19th century until today. Women and children are the real "others." In the early 20th century, "enlisted" photographers took photos of Arabs, in an effort to emphasize the Jews' good neighborly relations with the local residents. The current exhibit focuses on those whose exposure to the lens has always been minimal.

As a child, Silver-Brody saw how people treated "the other." Growing up in Rhodesia and visiting nearby South Africa, she was a witness to apartheid at its most extreme. Her parents were also affected by these discriminatory practices.

"In the early 1930s, my parents lived in Poland, near the German border," she relates. "When the Nazis came to power, they realized it was time to leave. My father traveled around Europe and bought a plot of land in Cyprus. In the end, he persuaded my mother to come with him to South Africa. A few weeks after they arrived, the country introduced new immigration laws." So the couple got on a train and left Capetown for Bulawayo, Rhodesia, where Vivienne was born. Her father opened a photography shop and specialized in portraits.

Years later, when Rhodesia was shaken by internal conflict, the father tried to persuade his wife and two daughters to move again, this time to Cyprus. In the end, they remained in Rhodesia, where the father died in 1974, a few months before the Turkish invasion of Cyprus, resulting in his property on the island losing its value.

Vivienne was already living in Israel by this time, working for Tel Aviv University, where she established a photo archive. To pursue her studies in the history of photography, she went to the United States. On her return, she says, she made up her mind to follow in the footsteps of American photographer Alfred Steiglitz, who "opened the eyes of the American public to photography as an art form."

`Good homes'

Steiglitz, they say, used to do background checks on clients who purchased prints from his galleries, to make sure that his work went to "good homes." Silver-Brody wanted her prints to end up in good homes, too, but she soon realized that in Israel, the problem is finding buyers to begin with.

"Israel doesn't have a tradition of collecting prints or hanging them in private homes," she says. "Not only does the public know nothing about many fascinating photographers who were active here in the past, but even photography students have never seen their work."

Silver-Brody began to delve into the legacy of photography in this country. She collected photographs from old archives and estates that no one had touched for years. Five years ago, she published a book entitled "Documenters of the Dream: Pioneer Jewish Photographers in the Land of Israel, 1890-1933."

In 2000, Silver-Brody curated an exhibit at the Mane Katz Museum: "From Mirror to Memory: A Hundred Years of Photography in the Land of Israel." The new millennium was the pretext for an almost impossible mission: summing up a century of photographic activity. Silver-Brody wondered how to present the society living in this country in all its different faces.

"At the beginning of the century, there were lots of photographic sources: Christian organizations, missionaries, pilgrims, Armenians, Jews, Arabs. But during certain periods, there is hardly any material, especially for the Arab sector in the 1930s and `40s and after the establishment of the state."

To fill in the blanks, Silver-Brody turned to amateur photography. "I made the rounds of Arab villages, Druze families and Christian schools, and discovered a real treasure trove," she says. These snapshots - among them pictures of a dance performance at the Italian school for girls in Haifa and the baptism of a newborn infant - were hung alongside the professional photographs at the exhibition. "I tried to include Muslim and Christian society in the documentation of events. As time goes on, it gets harder to find this kind of material."

Indeed, the store of photographs of interest to researchers is incomplete in a society that knowingly denies segments of its past - even events which have been documented. The archives of important photographers, among them Khalil Raad, are in Beirut, and the American Colony archives are in Washington. Silver-Brody says that Israel expressed no interest when the American Colony collection was put up for sale in the 1980s.

"Even the material we do have hasn't been sufficiently researched," says Silver-Brody, "and when research is inadequate, it's impossible to reach conclusions about what happened here. It takes courage, but confronting the past is something we have to do, and that includes the less pleasant aspects. One of the ways to do it is through photography."

Silver-Brody is one of the few people who hunt down such material and organize it for showing to the public, but she is cautious when it comes to conclusions. She is reluctant, for example, to label the major trends in Zionist photography at the beginning of the century "enlisted art."

"Most of the photographers who documented the realization of the Zionist dream believed in it themselves," she says. "The subjects may have been commissioned, but that doesn't mean the photographers were hired lackeys. If photography back then was `enlisted,' then what can you say about today, when money and boosting sales are the sacred values?

"We are talking about raw material," says Silver-Brody, when she talks about the history of the photography in "Eretz Israel." The same holds true for the current exhibit of photographs documenting women and children. "This show has no message whatsoever," she writes in a flyer distributed to visitors. "It is not political or feminist. It is simply a collection of quality photographs that explores one subject."

On one wall hangs a photograph that leaves the spectator feeling uneasy. It depicts an "Oriental" woman from the beginning of the century wearing traditional garb and laden with jewelry - a kind of anthropological exhibit. A modern equivalent might be those exotic pictures of "primitive," half-naked women, chiefly non-Western, taken in the interests of touristic documentation, without anyone asking their opinion.

Another photograph from the end of the 19th century shows a man driving a wagon in which his veiled wives are sitting. A rare photograph from the early 20th century shows the women's section of the Western Wall. On the stones one discerns the names of visitors, clearly marked in ink. This graffiti was later erased, on the orders of the British.

Next to an almost surrealistic photograph by Werner Braun, "Nuns Enjoying an Outing in the Valley of the Cross," hangs Boris Carmi's "Woman Officer," taken in 1948. Over the years, this has been one of the gallery's most popular prints. It seems to fit in with people's expectations of how the new Jewish woman should look.

"The tree that the soldier is leaning against, and the way that her body seems to repeat the shape of the tree, with its deep roots, is a symbol of the Zionist dream in all its glory," says Silver-Brody.

On closer inspection, one spies a number on the arm of a soldier in the foreground - taking the viewer back to the horrors and humiliation of the war. Not many notice this disturbing detail. Which goes to show that bringing these photographs to the public is not sufficient, even with heaps of commentary on the side. There has to be a public that is willing to take a closer look.

S. Africa steps up diplomacy on Iraq disarmament offer
By Reuters	| Feb. 15, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
CAPE TOWN - South Africa, interposing itself in an increasingly bitter debate over Iraq, said Saturday it was convinced its offer to share its expertise on disarmament with Baghdad could help avert war.

South Africa's offer, confirmed by President Thabo Mbeki in his state-of-the-nation speech on Friday, involves sending to Iraq a team of experts who led South Africa's own voluntary disarmament programme after the end of apartheid in 1994.

Mbeki said Iraq had accepted the offer and that the team, set to include specialists in chemical, biological and nuclear disarmament, would be sent shortly.

The diplomatic initiative comes as Pretoria takes the lead among developing nations in opposing a possible U.S. attack on Iraq, which it says would lead to economic disaster for Africa.

Deputy Foreign Minister Aziz Pahad said the South Africans would assist Iraq with the mechanics of cooperating with UN weapons inspectors - which he believed they were willing to do.

"We have no reason to question that," Pahad told reporters. "We are not presuming that they have weapons or they don't have weapons. It's a process of exchange of ideas while the inspectors carry out their task."

Pahad said South Africa's proposal had been put to members of the UN Security Council, including the United States and Britain, and had received "no negative response".

He said the South African team would include officials from the former apartheid government who helped build - and then destroy - the country's arsenal of weapons of mass destruction.

The South African offer came as the United States and Britain suffered a setback in the United Nations, where top UN arms officials on Friday held out the hope that arms inspections were working.

Pahad said South Africa, too, believed inspectors should be given more time, although he conceded the process should not be indefinite.

He said signs of Iraqi cooperation were promising, including its new ban on weapons of mass destruction and its permitting surveillance flights by U2 spy planes.

"There's a general concept that there have been quite substantial moves in trying to tackle the concerns of the Security Council," Pahad said. "Nothing supports the idea that we should go to war to achieve disarmament."


Home Print Edition Features
Not so simple to protest the war
The anti-Israel tone of current peace demonstrations raises a dilemma for American Jews.
By Nathan Guttman	| Feb. 17, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Ever since the 1960s, American Jews have been an inseparable part of the protest movement against war and for peace. Although this has not always been the official position of the Jewish establishment in the United States, the Jewish presence in the protest movements far exceeded their relative weight in the U.S. population.

The impending war in the Gulf is indicating for the first time that there is a fracture between American Jews and the opponents of war. Jewish activists who have been following the demonstrations and protest activities since last summer, cannot help but notice their evident anti-Israel line. Alongside the placards and exhortations against President George W. Bush, there are always placards attacking Israel and the occupation of the territories. Moreover, speakers at the demonstrations have often mentioned Israel as one of the key factors (alongside oil and his inheritance from his father) that are impelling President Bush to wage war against Iraq.

One of the reasons for this is that the main organization in the coalition of protest movements is ANSWER (Act Now to Stop War & End Racism), which has always had an aggressive anti-Israeli line. In its publications, the organization depicts Israel as "the other apartheid."

ANSWER's policy and the consistent anti-Israeli line in the protest demonstrations has caused a considerable number of American Jews who are against the war to stay away. Even those who have wanted to participate are not always welcome. Last week, Jewish leftist activist Rabbi Michael Lerner experienced this personally.

Rabbi Lerner, editor of the leftist Jewish periodical Tikkun and a severe critic of Prime Minister Ariel Sharon's government, was supposed to have spoken at an anti-war demonstration in San Francisco. Lerner, who is against a military campaign in Iraq, attacked ANSWER for "exploiting the demonstrations against the war to promote anti-Israeli propaganda." In an e-mail message he sent out after his appearance was canceled, Lerner stated that the organizers boycotted him because he refused to accept this policy. He wrote that he had intended to speak against the war but also against the anti-Semitism in the protest movement, and therefore he was not allowed to speak.

In the demonstrations last weekend, one Jewish speaker stood out in New York. This was Ruth Messinger, a veteran peace activist and formerly a Democratic candidate for mayor of New York. She herself also expressed reservations about the anti-Israeli line of the protest demonstrations, but said that she recognizes the fact that protest coalitions are made up of a variety of organizations with different agendas.

The Jewish establishment, through the Jewish Council for Public Affairs (JCPA), which constitutes a kind of political umbrella organization for Jewish bodies, has begun to act in recent weeks to change the tone in the protest organizations. JCPA representatives have contacted the various organizations, local and national, and have asked them to distinguish between protest against the war and protest against Israel and thus enable Jews and supporters of Israel to participate in the anti-war events. Response to this request has thus far been partial.

Ex-security chiefs present Israeli side of conflict in S. Africa
By Charlotte Hall	| Feb. 19, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
An Israeli delegation of former high-ranking security officials returned from Cape Town on Wednesday following four days of intensive talks with senior South African government and security officials, including those who served in the military wing of the ANC and the security establishment of the Apartheid government.

Four South African cabinet ministers were joined at the meeting by former and serving military and intelligence officers, who told the Israeli delegation about their experience of negotiations, peacemaking and the transition to democracy, says South African government spokeswoman Lorna Daniels.

South African president Thabo Mbeki also joined the final session of the four-day meet Monday, where he pledged to intensify his government's efforts to seek a peaceful resolution to the Israeli-Palestinian conflict.

The nine-strong unofficial Israeli delegation included former chief of staff and transport minister Amnon Lipkin-Shahak, former Northern District Police commander Alik Ron, Major General (Res.) Danny Rothschild and General (Res.) Uri Simhoni. Israeli military and security officials currently serving in the army and in government, were also invited by the South African government, but did not attend.

Rothschild said he felt by the end of the four days, the South African delegation "understood there are two sides to the coin" in the Arab-Israeli conflict. "We explained to them in detail that if they really wanted to take part in the peace process in the Middle East - as they say - they need to learn a lot and not take as fact what they hear from each side," he continued.

South African government spokeswoman Daniels agreed that the South Africans left the meetings with a better understanding of how Israelis view the conflict and the obstacles they face. She added that the interaction was helped by "the frank and constructive atmosphere." One inside source said it was the first time the former military leadership of the ANC, which has a historical affinity with the PLO, had met with senior Israelis.

The meeting, hosted by the South African government, was based at the Spier Wine Estate outside Stellenbosch near Cape Town. It grew up of a retreat at the same location a year ago, where Israeli peace activists and Palestinian Authority officials met for three days of intensive talks with President Mbeki and senior officials in the South African government.

Then too, discussions focused on the South African experience of conflict resolution and lessons that could be useful in the Middle East context.
