Home News
Palestinians urge sanctions against Israel
By Reuters	| Feb. 22, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
KUALA LUMPUR - A senior Palestinian official urged the Non-Aligned Movement on Saturday to use its influence in the United Nations to win the imposition of sanctions against Israel.

Israeli forces have carried out a series of armoured raids into the Gaza Strip in the past week, killing many Palestinians in attacks that have overshadowed new international peace efforts and roused fresh Palestinian fury.

"We expect more terrorism in this part of the world," Farouq al-Qaddoumi, head of the political directorate in the Palestinian Liberation Organisation, said on the sidelines of a summit of the 114 members of the Non-Aligned Movement (NAM) in the Malaysian capital, Kuala Lumpur.

Asked what NAM's members, who account for about two-thirds of the United Nations, could achieve at their three-yearly summit, al-Qaddoumi suggested sanctions to force Israel to give up what the Palestinians call "terrorism" against their people.

"NAM countries represent two-thirds of the countries of the world and have to shoulder the task in the UN of trying to impose sanctions - as they were imposed on the apartheid regime in South Africa," he said.

He did not give details of what type of sanctions he would want the developing nations - mainly from Africa, the Middle East and Asia - that comprise NAM to demand.

At this summit, South Africa is handing over chairmanship of the movement to Malaysia.

It was not clear whether the summit would take up such a call during a meeting dominated by discussion on how to tackle weapons of mass destruction, particularly the U.S. threat of war on Iraq and North Korea's nuclear ambitions.

NAM calls for Iraq disarmament without war Members of the Non-Aligned Movement called Saturday for Iraq to surrender any weapons of mass destruction but the countries that account for two-thirds of the United Nations rejected war as the means to disarmament.

Opening their three-yearly ministerial meeting in the Malaysian capital, NAM members said there was no question that Iraq must comply with UN resolutions but they strongly opposed any U.S.-led war against Iraq.

"Iraq must be disarmed if it possesses weapons of mass destruction, but the question is how best to achieve it," said South African Foreign Minister Nkosazana Dlamini-zuma.

"We believe that it is possible and desireable that we do this through peaceful means," she said. "We must assert the centrality of the United Nations in settling such matters."

The issue of weapons of mass destruction in Iraq and North Korea has dominated the run-up to a two-day summit Monday and Tuesday of leaders of the 114-member NAM.

The United States and Britain are massing 150,000 troops on Iraq's border, threatening war unless President Saddam Hussein surrenders alleged stockpiles of weapons of mass destruction.

Foreign ministers from NAM are set later Saturday to approve a resolution that "categorically rejects the current threat of war".

Iraq seized the opportunity to lobby furiously for support to counter the United States in the final declaration.

"We hope this statement truly expresses the feelings of the peoples of NAM, totally rejecting the idea of aggression, hegemony and war planned by Washington," Iraqi Foreign Minister Naji Sabri told reporters.

But the tone of the gathering did not go entirely Iraq's way.

The grouping, which includes Iran, Iraq and North Korea - the three states branded by U.S. President George W. Bush as an "axis of evil" - has rejected a request by Baghdad to deny Washington use of their soil for any Middle East war.

Ministers have been fiercely critical of the United States, with several in their speeches taking swipes at what they called "the unilateral use of force".

Malaysian Foreign Minister Abdullah Badawi said the growing influence of "just one superpower" had brought the world to the brink of conflict:

"A conflict which the peoples of the world have loudly and clearly opposed," he said, adding that the collective voice of the global majority had said "no to war".

Iraq has welcomed the support it has received from the movement that was founded during the Cold War as a counterweight to the Eastern and Western blocs.

Baghdad says it is doing everything it can to cooperate with UN inspectors combing the country for weapons.

Ministers have said the UN inspectors, headed by Hans Blix, should be given ample time to make a proper assessment and not coaxed into accelerating the drive to war.

U.S. ally Pakistan was among those opposed to war and willing to consider more time for UN inspections, but insisted that Iraq must comply with United Nations resolutions on disarming.

"All means must be found to avoid war, if that means more time has to be given to the inspectors," Foreign Minister Khursheed Mehmood Kasuri told reporters. "And there's that report from Mr Blix, let's wait for that report."

Members have stressed the weight of NAM's views.

United Arab Emirates envoy Mohamad Jassam reminded the United States that six non-aligned countries currently have seats on the 15-member UN Security Council and that nine votes would be enough to kill a resolution.

The six non-aligned nations on the Security Council are Angola, Guinea, Syria, Pakistan, Chile and Cameroon.

Malaysia expects 56 heads of state to attend the two-day summit, with several Middle East nations sending substitutes due to uncertainty over the timing of an emergency Arab Summit in Egypt to discuss the Iraq crisis.

No consensus on North Korea Apparent agreement over the question of war with Iraq was not matched by consensus on the North Korean nuclear crisis.

Pyongyang was set to snub the group by sticking to its position that it would discuss its nuclear ambitions with the United States alone and disagreeing with requests from its 113 fellow members that it reconsider its decision to quit the nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty (NPT).

The working group on disarmament failed to win North Korea's agreement to a statement opposing Pyongyang's decision last month to withdraw from the NPT and moved instead to raise the deadlock to a higher level, an Indonesian delegate said.

Poverty-stricken communist North Korea has kicked out weapons inspectors and left the NPT, saying it needs nuclear arms and wants to discuss the crisis with the United States. Washington says it will hold multilateral talks.


Home News
Letters to the Editor
Mar. 13, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
International law has clearly formulated rules as to the conduct of armed conflicts by states, and these rules are set out in the hundreds of regulations detailed in various treaties, including those in the four 1949 Geneva Conventions. However, on the question as to when a state may use force in its international relations, international law is vague, and the answer is contained only in a few generalized principles.

The United Nations Charter outlaws the use of force between states, and it is claimed that this is now a rule of customary international law - if so, it is, in Hamlet's words, "a custom more honour'd in the breach than the observance." The UN Charter language forbids "the use of force against the territorial integrity or political independence of any State, or in any other manner inconsistent with the Purposes of the United Nations." This choice of language leaves open the interpretation that use of force is permitted, if it is in furtherance of the purposes of the UN.

The UN Charter also states that "Nothing in the present Charter shall impair the inherent right of individual or collective self-defense if an armed attack occurs." In regard to the possible attack against Iraq, the U.S. claim is, inter alia, that it exercised a right of self-defense against Iraq's attacks on Israel, Kuwait and Saudi Arabia, and that in light of Iraq's threats and behavior, that right of self-defense continues to be valid. International law permits a state to act in self-defense not only when it itself is attacked but also when, at the request of an attacked state, it comes to the aid of that state. Self-defense can be activated in response to preliminary activity of an attack, and a state is not required to wait passively until bombs actually fall. It is claimed that states are even entitled to take preemptive action to frustrate a possible attack though this interpretation is controversial.

In addition to the right of self-defense, international law recognizes that states are entitled to act out of necessity. Necessity can allow states to take actions, including the use of force, which, if it were not for such necessity, would be violations of international law. Such action is permitted in order to prevent a more serious violation of international law that would affect a vital interest of the state. An example of such action was the British Royal Air Force's bombing of the oil tanker Torrey Canyon. The ship broke up off the English coast, and if it hadn't been destroyed, it would have caused immense ecological harm. Another example came, in 1965, when Israel shelled the Syrian tractors working on the Arab diversion program intended to deprive Israel of the use of the waters of the river Jordan. There was no armed attack by Syria against Israel, but Israel's action was a clear case of necessity.

Since Iraq has not complied with Security Council decisions on disarming its weapons of mass destruction, U.S. action to disarm Iraq could well be considered as an act of necessity. It is problematic whether U.S. action aimed at replacing the Iraqi regime could also come under this rubric. Actions by states involving the right of self-defense or acts of necessity do not require Security Council approval.

Beyond the rights of states to self-defense and necessity, the UN Charter grants the Security Council powers of activating collective security when the Council determines that there has occurred an act of "aggression," a "threat to the peace" or a "breach of the peace." This right of collective security is, in fact, a modern application of the principle of the balance of power dating back to Metternich and the 1815 Congress of Vienna and its nexus to international law is purely formal. Because of the right of veto granted to the five permanent members of the Council, there has been scarce use of collective security measures and there have been no Security Council resolutions taking mandatory measures regarding the numerous armed conflicts that have taken place since the foundation of the UN.

Claims are heard that the international law applies a double standard as regards Iraq's non-compliance with Security Council resolutions compared with that applied to Israel. Such claims ignore a basic principle of the UN Charter as regards Security Council decisions. According to the Charter the only Council decisions that are binding are those adopted under Chapter VII, which deals with a determination that there has been aggression or a threat or breach of the peace. All other decisions have the status of recommendations. The decisions as regards Iraq were adopted under Chapter VII, and are mandatory, but since 1948 no decision as regards the Arab-Israel conflict has been adopted under Chapter VII. All the numerous decisions, including Security Council Resolution 242, had the status of recommendations and of course the question of violating them does not arise.

Robbie Sabel

Robbie Sabel teaches international law at the Hebrew University and is a former legal adviser to the Ministry of Foreign Affairs.

I will not participate in my upcoming reserve duty

For over 35 years, using its military might, Israel has been occupying the West Bank and the Gaza Strip. About 3.5 million Palestinians are under its occupation. An entire nation has been marked as the enemy. Millions of people, different from one another, have been painted in the Israeli consciousness in colors of terror and inhumanity, to make it easier for us to justify our behavior, to continue to avert our glance - mainly so we can continue to be occupiers.

But these people, the subjects of the occupation, are not abstract figures. I saw the two teenage boys who were taken from the yard of their house, handcuffed, kneeling in the deep mud. I saw an older man struggling with his trembling lower lip, in order not to cry after 19-year-old soldiers decided to educate him, and to delay him at the checkpoint for an entire day. I saw a woman, on a particularly cold and rainy winter day, swaddling her baby and waiting, ID card in hand, until the soldier had time to motion to her with his finger to approach for a check. I knew the man whose nephew was killed because soldiers chose him to serve as a human shield for them. I spoke to people who called an ambulance to evacuate the wounded and the sick, but it didn't arrive because our forces prevented its passage. I met the family that collected rainwater all winter, until someone in the Civil Administration decided to destroy the reservoir, since they had no permits. I read the words of the man who was forced to evacuate the body of his neighbor, and the story of the old wife of the village idiot, whom everyone knew and pitied, until he was shot by soldiers.

I can only be ashamed and try to protest. Occupation, any occupation, depends on the willingness of soldiers to maintain it. I know that many soldiers, in the standing army and in the reserves, are still willing to carry out their orders; to continue to believe, despite what they see with their own eyes, that repression can lead to victory. I won't be among them. I refuse to be an active partner to what is being done in my name in any case.

There are deeds that are unforgivable. We will all have to answer for the killing of civilians, the mass destruction of homes, the curfew, the humiliation and the starvation; for the policy of apartheid that distinguishes between the Jewish master and his Palestinian subject; for the out-and-out racism and the hard-heartedness.

I have been working in a human rights organization for about a year-and-a-half. In my work, I am exposed daily to Israel's routinely negative conduct in the territories. I am witness to the military's mobilization for carrying out and preparing these deeds. In the course of my work, I frequently travel to the occupied territories, learn at first-hand about the situation I have described, and try to change it. I won't go to those places in uniform.

Eyal Raz

The writer was sentenced this week to 14 days in jail for refusing to serve in the territories.

Austria's effort

Regarding the opinion piece "Vienna doesn't care any more" by Adar Primor, March 10:

I very much appreciate your interest in political events in Vienna and do agree with many aspects of your analysis, in particular regarding the outcome of the last parliamentary elections in Austria and the achievements of the previous Austrian government, which "did more than any other government to deal with its past."

In this context, I would like to add that Austria has indeed successfully intensified its efforts to come to grips with Austrians' involvement in crimes of the Nazi terror regime and to urgently address remaining restitution payments to victims.

Back in 1998, Austria established an international historians' commission, which recently presented its final report on the seizure of property on Austrian territory under the Nazis and restitution since 1945. Furthermore, Austria is very much obligated to human rights education and to pass on to its young generation the devastating truth of the darker period in its history, the Holocaust. Among the central aspects of this educational program are regular visits of Austrian schoolteachers to Yad Vashem.

On the other hand, I cannot but stress that neither the Austrian government nor this embassy share Mr. Sichrovsky's assessment as contained in the last paragraph of Primor's article and used in the headline "Vienna doesn't care anymore."

Throughout the past two years - while political relations have been strained - we have continuously aimed at improving bilateral relations between Austria and Israel, inter alia in the cultural, economic and social fields.

We will continue to further address this great and promising potential with a view to establishing cordial relations between our two countries and peoples. Full diplomatic relations - with an Ambassador of the State of Israel in Austria - are an important prerequisite in this regard.

Dr. Kurt Hengel

Austrian Ambassador in Israel

Fair balance

Regarding "Eitan, Bar-On slam justice appointment process" by Gideon Alon, March 25:

In his article, Gideon Alon sheds much needed light on the Supreme Court in Israel, which wields tremendous power, yet does not represent both sides of the political spectrum. Here in the U.S., the federal and supreme courts are made up of judges who were nominated and appointed by presidents who were conservative and liberal, Democrat and Republican, so there is a mixture of conservatives and liberals on the bench, who each reflect the majority of the population who elected those administrations. That is why we now have a conservative attorney general, John Ashcroft, who is deeply religious and very strong and aggressive in dealing with the threat of terrorism America now faces. It is undemocratic and shameful that the entire court system and Ministry of Justice in Israel is made up exclusively of secular, liberal judges and officials, who do not reflect the majority of the population who voted for a conservative government. Why are conservatives not represented in the Israeli court system, and civil and state administrations, for that matter, as they are in every other democratic country?

Marlene Young

Baltimore, MD

Herzliya Pituah

WHO's `health apartheid' for Taiwan

Regarding "Mysterious pneumonia is becoming worldwide health threat, WHO warns" by A

According to its constitution, the goal of the World Health Organization (WHO) is "the attainment by all peoples of the highest possible level of health." However, this noble spirit of the constitution has been compromised and the name of this professional medical organization thus tarnished, as not all peoples are under its protection umbrella.

The WHO now has listed 192 members, two associate members (Puerto Rico and Tokelau) and 29 areas (including Hong Kong and Macao and places like the Cook Islands and the Falkland Islands) on its Web site's (www.who.int/ith/indexofcountries.html) International Travel and Health pages. Taiwan, whose population of 23 million is larger than 75 percent of those member countries, is excluded. This is "health apartheid" against Taiwan that should be addressed and rectified in no time.

China has put forth a number of arguments against Taiwan's entry, one of which is that Taiwan has no statehood at all, and the other is that there is no need for Taiwan's participation in the WHO because China looks after Taiwan's health interests. Nothing can be further from the truth. Taiwan is a 100 percent sovereign state, and China has never contributed in any way to the health needs of Taiwan. And contagious diseases don't recognize if you are a sovereign state or not.

In the year 2002, Taiwan registered 7.85 million outbound travelers and 2.19 million visitors. By the end of 2002, more than 300,000 migrant workers from five Southeast Asian countries were living and working in Taiwan. Taiwan is a bustling international trading center with $243 billion in foreign trade in 2002. The contact with China has been increasing. This combination of factors places Taiwan at the crossroads of any infectious disease outbreak in the region, like the current mysterious pneumonia; outbreaks have been reported in China, Hong Kong and Singapore, and unconfirmed new cases were reported in Vietnam and Taiwan.

Taiwan needs to have real-time information provided by the WHO to combat fatal disease and vice-versa.

In 2000, a survey in the British Economist Intelligence Unit rated medical practice in Taiwan as being second among all the developed countries and newly industrialized countries, next only to Sweden. From 1995 to 2002, Taiwan donated to the international community over $120 million in medical and humanitarian relief to 78 countries spanning five continents, plus $20 million to war-torn Afghanistan. Recently, Taiwan also donated $1 million to the Global Fund to Fight AIDS, tuberculosis, and malaria. In a nutshell, Taiwan should be an asset, not a liability, to the WHO. Why not accept it?

On August 17, 1999, a big earthquake devastated Turkey, yet Taiwan's experienced medical teams were not allowed to assist the rescue efforts. On September 21, 1999, a massive earthquake struck central Taiwan, killing 2,400 people and leaving more than 10,000 injured. China used its diplomatic muscle to obstruct foreign rescue assistance. Such inhumane practice would not have occurred were Taiwan a member of the WHO. The racial apartheid of the old days in South Africa is a disgrace in human history. The "health apartheid" against Taiwan is another shameful blow to the human race, one the international community has to take action to rectify now.

Inviting Taiwan to participate as an observer in sessions of the World Health Assembly (WHA) to be held this year from May 19 to 28 in Geneva is the first step. China should seize this opportunity to demonstrate its willingness to look after Taiwan's health interests. People in Taiwan have been shut out by a closed door long enough. It is high time for a change.

David Tzou

Taipei Economic and Cultural Office in Tel Aviv

WHO's `health apartheid' for Taiwan Regarding "Mysterious pneumonia is becoming worldwide health threat, WHO warns" by Associated Press correspondent in Hanoi, March 16, 2003

According to its constitution, the goal of the World Health Organization (WHO) is "the attainment by all peoples of the highest possible level of health." However, this noble spirit of the constitution has been compromised and the name of this professional medical organization thus tarnished, as not all peoples are under its protection umbrella.

The WHO now has listed 192 members, two associate members (Puerto Rico and Tokelau) and 29 areas (including Hong Kong and Macao and places like the Cook Islands and the Falkland Islands) on its Web site's (www.who.int/ith/indexofcountries.html) International Travel and Health pages. Taiwan, whose population of 23 million is larger than 75 percent of those member countries, is excluded. This is "health apartheid" against Taiwan that should be addressed and rectified in no time.

China has put forth a number of arguments against Taiwan's entry, one of which is that Taiwan has no statehood at all, and the other is that there is no need for Taiwan's participation in the WHO because China looks after Taiwan's health interests. Nothing can be further from the truth. Taiwan is a 100 percent sovereign state, and China has never contributed in any way to the health needs of Taiwan. And contagious diseases don't recognize if you are a sovereign state or not.

In the year 2002, Taiwan registered 7.85 million outbound travelers and 2.19 million visitors. By the end of 2002, more than 300,000 migrant workers from five Southeast Asian countries were living and working in Taiwan. Taiwan is a bustling international trading center with $243 billion in foreign trade in 2002. The contact with China has been increasing. This combination of factors places Taiwan at the crossroads of any infectious disease outbreak in the region, like the current mysterious pneumonia; outbreaks have been reported in China, Hong Kong and Singapore, and unconfirmed new cases were reported in Vietnam and Taiwan.

Taiwan needs to have real-time information provided by the WHO to combat fatal disease and vice-versa.

In 2000, a survey in the British Economist Intelligence Unit rated medical practice in Taiwan as being second among all the developed countries and newly industrialized countries, next only to Sweden. From 1995 to 2002, Taiwan donated to the international community over $120 million in medical and humanitarian relief to 78 countries spanning five continents, plus $20 million to war-torn Afghanistan. Recently, Taiwan also donated $1 million to the Global Fund to Fight AIDS, tuberculosis, and malaria. In a nutshell, Taiwan should be an asset, not a liability, to the WHO. Why not accept it?

On August 17, 1999, a big earthquake devastated Turkey, yet Taiwan's experienced medical teams were not allowed to assist the rescue efforts. On September 21, 1999, a massive earthquake struck central Taiwan, killing 2,400 people and leaving more than 10,000 injured. China used its diplomatic muscle to obstruct foreign rescue assistance. Such inhumane practice would not have occurred were Taiwan a member of the WHO. The racial apartheid of the old days in South Africa is a disgrace in human history. The "health apartheid" against Taiwan is another shameful blow to the human race, one the international community has to take action to rectify now.

Inviting Taiwan to participate as an observer in sessions of the World Health Assembly (WHA) to be held this year from May 19 to 28 in Geneva is the first step. China should seize this opportunity to demonstrate its willingness to look after Taiwan's health interests. People in Taiwan have been shut out by a closed door long enough. It is high time for a change.

David Tzou Taipei Economic and Cultural Office in Tel Aviv

Home News
ANC gets crucial 2/3 majority in S.Africa assembly
By Reuters	| Apr. 3, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
JOHANNESBURG - South Africa's ruling African National Congress (ANC) has obtained a two-thirds majority in parliament - a crucial level of support needed to change the country's constitution.

The magic threshold was reached this week during the latest round of musical chairs by sitting members of parliament.

Under a controversial law that took effect last month, MPs can defect from one party to another during specific time-periods without seeking a new mandate from voters.

ANC chief whip Nkosinathi Nhleko said on Thursday the party had gained nine defectors via "floor-crossing," bringing its total seats to 275, or 68.75 percent of the 400-seat parliament.

The ANC had held 66 percent, or 266 seats, since the 1999 general elections - just under the threshold.

"The realignments signify the fact that South African society is reaching consensus about the correctness of ANC policies," Nhleko said in a statement.

But opposition parties criticized the way the ANC came by its seats, saying the matter should be decided democratically in elections. The next general elections will be held in 2004.

A two-thirds majority is required to change the constitution and its key bill of rights.

But any changes must also be in line with the bill of rights and must not contradict other parts of the constitution. If they do, they risk being struck down by the Constitutional Court, the highest court in South Africa, legal experts said.

The ANC has not signaled any plans to change the constitution, and Nhleko said despite the party's gains, it would strive to reach consensus on critical issues.

"It is an emotional thing. The ANC believes it has the backing of the overwhelming majority of the people and it was an anomaly that was not reflected in parliament," said Mogale Mashiapata of the Institute for Multiparty Democracy.

The ANC narrowly failed to reach the magic two-thirds limit in South Africa's first all-race elections in 1994, which marked the end of apartheid and white minority rule, and again in 1999.

Critics have condemned floor-crossing as contempt for the electorate and portrayed the ANC as power hungry.

"Floor-crossing downgrades democracy. There is nothing democratic about a small elite class going against the wishes of the electorate and unilaterally changing parties," said Reverend Musa Zondi, spokesman of the Inkatha Freedom Party (IFP).

The IFP is small nationally but controls the volatile and populous eastern KwaZulu-Natal province, the scene of sporadic violence pitting the IFP against the ANC in the last decade.


Home Print Edition Opinion
An unholy alliance with the Christian right
The annual conference of the powerful pro-Israel AIPAC lobby last week disproved the conspiracy theory that claimed the Jews persuaded President Bush to conquer Iraq. According to the same theory, the lobby is now pressing him not to present the road map to put an end to the Israeli occupation of the territories.
By Akiva Eldar	| Apr. 7, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
The annual conference of the powerful pro-Israel AIPAC lobby last week disproved the conspiracy theory that claimed the Jews persuaded President Bush to conquer Iraq. According to the same theory, the lobby is now pressing him not to present the road map to put an end to the Israeli occupation of the territories.

On the first day of the AIPAC convention, a man named Gary Bauer took the podium. He reminded the cheering thousands that God gave the Land of Israel to the Jewish people and, therefore, there is an absolute ban on giving it to another people. Bauer is not a member of the National Religious Party, nor of the Likud central committee. He's not even Jewish. He is a leading preacher from the Christian right in America, one of those who believe the Jews are The Chosen People and one day will even choose the right messiah. Bauer is a leading spokesman for arch-conservative policies, including a total ban on all abortions and favoring government funding for religious schools.

These are the people generating the spiritual energy fueling George Bush's war on global terrorism. Evangelist Christians from South Carolina paid for the huge billboard on the Ayalon Highway declaring "There's no land for peace." TV evangelist Pat Robertson last week reprimanded Foreign Minister Silvan Shalom, saying "Who do you think you are, handing Jerusalem over to Arafat?"

With Christian friends like these close to the president's ear, the right-wing government in Israel does not need Jewish friends to rebuff political initiatives like the road map. But the Jewish activists are not giving up. The religious sources of the values that drive the Christian right are not preventing some Jewish organizations from turning them into a natural ally. Among those organizations are some that only a decade ago were thriving by exposing the anti-Semitic sloganeering in the sermons of some of their newfound friends.

This coming Passover, those Jews will devotedly recite "Next year in Jerusalem rebuilt," and a few might even do so from one of the hotels in the capital, which have been empty for the last two years. Those same activists joining the crusade against renewal of the political negotiations and against a settlement freeze know what a bloody price Israel is paying for the conflict in the territories. They are familiar with the ominous economic data threatening the social stability of their beloved country. They all understand that by the end of this decade, the Jews will become a minority between the Jordan and Mediterranean.

So what drives these Jewish professionals? A new poll for one of the Jewish organizations shows that their policy does not represent the Jewish street in America. According to this poll, 63 percent of American Jewry supports active involvement by the U.S. administration in the peace process. This could confirm the assessment of one senior Israeli diplomat, who noted that the name Jay Fielder, a young Jewish football player, is much better known to American Jews than that of Malcolm Hoenlein, the eternal executive vice-chairman of the Conference of Presidents of Major American Jewish Organizations.

It is not because, as in Israel, the majority supports left-wing concessions but allows the political leadership to lead right-wing policies. The big difference between the two communities remains that Israeli Jews get blown up in buses, their sons have to guard settlers and their grandchildren can expect to grow up in a binational state or an apartheid regime. If it is difficult for those American Jewish busybodies to push the president and Congress into the cold water of the peace process, presumably one could expect they not try to force the administration to go in the opposite direction. They even have the right to draw fire to the Jews over the Iraq war, but they do not have the right to block even the slightest chance for peace here.


Days of Ubunto
By Tom Segev	| Apr. 14, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
It all began at Bergen-Belsen

The first two people I spoke with about national reconciliation in South Africa were murderers; the third is the current minister of education. "When did you first hear the term Ubuntu," I asked Ntobeko Penni, who answered that he'd heard his father use it when he was six years old. "Ubuntu" - humanness in Zulu - is an African outlook on life, the essence of which is that a person is a person through other people. Some say this perspective on life is largely what led to the foundation of the Truth and Reconciliation Commission (TRC), after apartheid's collapse in the latter half of the 1990s.

Penni, 28, was one of four young black men who murdered Amy Biehl, a young American woman who came to South Africa to show her solidarity with the black struggle. Penni and his comrades attacked her in the street and tore her to shreds.

Education Minister Kader Asmal said he only heard of Ubuntu in the past few years, after the term began to be used by the media as part of an attempt to root the TRC in the African tradition.

Asmal is a short, mustachioed man of 68, a Muslim of Indian descent, who looks and sounds like a British professor. No wonder - a graduate of the London School of Economics, he was not permitted to return to his homeland, and spent the next 25 years teaching law at Trinity College in Dublin, Ireland. Asmal was born in Natal province; his father was a shopkeeper. When Asmal was 11, he told me, he sneaked into a whites-only cinema. It was 1945. He doesn't remember what film was shown, but the newsreel that day changed his life. It was about the British Army's arrival at the Bergen-Belsen concentration camp. He has never forgotten, and never will, the images of bulldozers clearing away piles of bodies, said Asmal. Afterward, he hurried to tell his mother that despite her wishes, he would not be an imam, but that he wanted to study law, so he could help those subjected to political persecution. And so he did. His legal and political activities led to his exile.

While living in Britain and Ireland, said Asmal, he spent a lot of time studying the history of Nazi Germany, the annihilation of the Jews and the Nuremberg trials. The more he learned, the more he expanded his political activity, and became one of the chief spokesmen in Europe against apartheid. One dream kept him going: Someday the evil regime in South Africa would collapse, and he would return home and devote himself to bringing to trial the criminals of the white regime; it would be an African Nuremberg trial.

In September 1990, Asmal was permitted to return. He accepted a university post as a professor of human rights, and began preparing for the trials he'd been envisioning for all those years. But Oliver Tambo, the legendary leader of the African National Congress (ANC), the leading underground group, decided there would be no trials in South Africa akin to those held for Nazi criminals, as such trials were vindictive, and were liable to tear up South Africa instead of uniting and building it. South Africa needed reconciliation, not revenge, he said. Other people involved in the establishment of the TRC confirm Asmal's story - up to here. Nor is there any argument that Tambo's and especially Nelson Mandela's wisdom and forethought, prevented Nuremberg-style "trials of revenge."

According to Asmal, Tambo asked him to think of another way to expose the truth about the crimes of the white regime. After some thought, Asmal suggested that instead of a trial, the government set up a public commission of inquiry. At first they wanted to call it the "Truth Commission," but in order to prevent any critics from dwelling on the Orwellian origins of the name, they settled on "Truth and Reconciliation Commission." The idea was implemented after much discussion, to which many voices contributed. One was attorney Albie Sachs, another champion of human rights.

Mama said

Sachs, of Jewish descent, is now 67. He was arrested during the apartheid era for his activities on behalf of the ANC. He was held in isolation and eventually forced out of his country. At first he settled in England, but later moved to Mozambique, where as a law professor he continued his anti-apartheid activities. The South African authorities must have felt that he had strong influence; they dispatched secret agents to Mozambique, who planted a bomb in his car. Sachs was miraculously saved, but the intensity of the blast caused him to lose his right arm and one eye. He, too, went home in 1990, and he now serves as a justice of the Constitutional Court.

The Truth and Reconciliation Commission, Sachs told me, was not born so as to settle accounts with the white regime, but as the result of the need to deal with crimes of the ANC itself. The story begins with a session of the ANC's National Executive Committee in August 1993, held about eight months before the first-ever democratic elections in South Africa. It was a difficult meeting. The issue was how to deal with a report of an internal commission set up by the ANC to expose the barbaric interrogation methods used by its members against collaborators with the apartheid regime. Aside from the horrific tortures administered during interrogations, ANC men would detain suspected collaborators, place tires around their necks, and set them aflame. Sachs is proud that the ANC made the decision to investigate itself. It may be the only liberation movement to ever have done so, he says.

However, the feeling of self-satisfaction was not enough; the findings were atrocious, and something clearly had to be done. Discussion of the report continued for a long time. It was embarrassing, as the ANC was poised to assume control and lead South Africa into a new era. Finally, one of the participants stood up and asked simply, "Let us think for a moment what my mama would say about it." When he said `my mama' he meant the healthy logic of a simple, unsophisticated woman who may be unaware of politics but is blessed with a robust sense of decency and justice.

The speaker then offered the answer: "We are simply crazy. Here we are, examining our own weaknesses and faults, and exposing our nakedness to the whole world - which maybe has to be done - while in the meantime those rascals and villains on the other side, who for decades and centuries murdered, mutilated and tortured our people, are getting away scot-free." The others nodded their heads, and then Kader Asmal proposed a truth commission, which would expose all of the evil in its entirety, of all the evildoers, whites and non-whites alike.

Previously, there had been much discussion in South Africa about the possibility of setting up a forum for clarifying the historic truth. Committees had been formed, surveys held, experts from other countries consulted. There was a study of how other countries, such as Argentina, Chile and Zimbabwe, had handled the issue.

The upheaval

The apartheid regime evolved over the years and did not collapse all at once. It gradually crumbled, primarily, it seems, as a result of the international sanctions enacted against South Africa. The breakup of the Soviet Union nullified the main factor that justified the apartheid regime, besides the racist ideology itself. Many whites truly believed that the evil regime was necessary to protect their country from the Communist threat. In retrospect, everyone now speaks of ideological laxity, fatigue, a general feeling of "we can't go on this way" among the whites. The governing party had become a doormat, the economic situation deteriorated. There was a feeling that everything was falling apart, and masses of people were leaving the country.

In the 1980's, the authorities began to cancel some regulations of "the petty apartheid," which forbade blacks from sitting in public parks on white-only benches, or using telephone booths or public restrooms designated for use by whites. However, the fundamentals of discrimination, including the lack of a parliamentary vote for blacks, were supposed to remain unchanged. But the willingness to nibble at the foundations of apartheid seems to have done to South Africa what perestroika did to the Soviet Union. The authorities were unable to control the narrow cracks that opened in the wall of repression, the cracks grew wider and the walls fell. The journalist and commentator Vessel de Kock explained to me that as more black workers were being allowed to live in close proximity to white-owned factories, over time a mixed economy developed: The political system was no longer suited to the economic situation and therefore had to be changed. Meanwhile, the ANC was growing stronger.

Colin Eglin, a veteran parliamentarian, told me that Frederik Willem de Klerk, the last white president, decided to hold talks with the blacks shortly before he lost everything. Nelson Mandela accepted the offer shortly before he was to win everything. The result was a uniquely realistic compromise. Many whites in South Africa, if not all, and numerous foreign observers, assumed that with the collapse of apartheid, the blacks would rise up, drive out the whites, and slaughter any that stayed behind, to avenge centuries of racism, during which the evil was, at its height, equivalent to that of the Nazis. That this did not happen is referred to by some as "the miracle of South Africa."

I spent some time asking people to explain this "miracle" to me, but I eventually stopped. The question itself shows a prejudice, journalist Zubeida Jaffer told me. We aren't a primitive tribe of man-eaters. She suggested I read the ANC publications dating back to its inception in the early 20th century, and especially the freedom charter it adopted in the early `50s. The movement had never threatened to kill the whites. On the contrary, it had always called for reestablishing life on the basis of full equality, calling for South Africa to be a state of all its citizens, blacks and whites and all the others too.

Jaffer is a well-known political commentator. We sailed to Robben Island, where Mandela was held prisoner for 18 of the 27 years of his incarceration. On the way there, she spoke of her own arrest and torture. As a talented reporter with good sources, she was able to report exclusive news about the black struggle. The authorities suspected her of belonging to an underground organization, and had her arrested. The interrogators threatened to rape her - but did not do so, although they did beat her, and forced her to swallow a drug that caused nausea, and pain all over her body. At the time of her second arrest, she was pregnant. The interrogators threatened to "burn her fetus," but she was released before her daughter's birth. Only recently has she begun to recover. Prior to her arrest, Jaffer was allowed to visit prisoners held on Robben Island together with Mandela. The current visit to the prison as a tourist, with me, provoked strange feelings.

Island of Good Hope

Robben Island has served as a place of detention almost from the day Vasco de Gama dropped anchor there 500 years ago, as a quarantine camp for slaves and lepers, and a prison for criminal convicts and political opponents. Some of the men imprisoned together with Mandela now serve as guides, sharing their memories with the tourists. At the height of the tour, the visitor arrives at Mandela's cell.

Conditions were harsh, and the wardens abusive. Mandela and his comrades spent part of the time working in a nearby quarry; the dazzling light severely damaged his eyesight. The long years on Robben Island had an effect on his worldview: the prolonged struggle for improving prison conditions made him more acutely realistic, taught him to better assess what can and cannot be achieved, judge when to be stubborn and when to yield.

The more the whites withdrew from their positions, the more the security forces demanded that any settlement be conditioned upon a blanket amnesty for anyone involved in the crimes of apartheid, including expulsions, liquidations, faked terrorist attacks, torture, murder, rape, plunder and corruption. At one stage, the talks nearly broke down over this demand. Several army leaders threatened to start a civil war. On this point, the nation's jurists, including Justice Minister Dullah Omar, seem to have played a significant role. Albie Sachs' wisdom and prestige also aided in resolving this disagreement.

A blanket amnesty was out of the question, of course, Sachs explained to me. But the controversy threatened the acceptance of the new constitution, which may be the best and most righteous constitution in the world. Its acceptance saved South Africa from a civil war and perhaps from complete destruction. Sachs feels the constitution was worth conceding on the sensitive issue of granting amnesty to apartheid criminals. After much effort, the sides reached a compromise: no blanket amnesty - instead, separate discussion of each and every case as part of the TRC hearings. Along with granting amnesty, the commission was also authorized to rule on compensation to victims of the regime.

Amnesty seekers had to comply with two main conditions: First, they had to disclose the truth about their crimes, although they were not required to express remorse. Second, they had to prove that their acts were performed as part of their political position. This was new: Most people accused of crimes against humanity defend themselves by arguing that "they were only carrying out orders." This line of defense has not been accepted, partially because of the duty of refusing a blatantly illegal order. In South Africa, they took the opposite course: Anyone who could prove he committed the acts while serving the country, or even while serving its political ideology, qualified for amnesty. Many of those harmed by apartheid, especially the families of its victims, had a hard time accepting the idea of criminals going unpunished. Sachs is not happy with the amnesty arrangement, either, even though he had much influence on its acceptance.

Sachs told me about the day that a man named Henry appeared in his chambers. A former South African army officer, he told Sachs that he had been involved in gathering the intelligence prior to the booby-trapping of Sachs' car. Sachs tried to draw him out: He was an excellent officer who evidently believed he was dispatching a dangerous enemy. They sat together for nearly two hours. "He looked at me almost with a measure of jealousy. I was a judge of the Constitutional Court, and he had been cast aside and was unemployed. He too had injuries, he told me. He had been shot in the leg and walked with a slight limp. We could have gone on eyeing each other and talking forever. I stood up and said: `Henry, (a cheap emotion surged in me. I was tempted to say: `I cannot shake your hand. You know why.') I cannot shake your hand now. Go to the TRC, tell them your story, do something for South Africa and then maybe we'll meet again.' He looked so uncomfortable, uneasy and sad when he left."

Henry is one of thousands of witnesses who have appeared before the commission and requested amnesty. The two men eventually did see each other again, at which time Sachs shook the other's hand. Sachs did not ask if Henry had received amnesty. He thinks he did. The number of amnesty recipients is relatively low: about 2,000 of the 7,000 who applied for it. The granting of amnesty has not added much luster to the commission's prestige, and in retrospect, along with its successes it has had its weaknesses. Mary Burton, a member of the commission, told me about them.


Home Print Edition Business
Continuation of Days of Ubunto
Apr. 14, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
We are all guilty

She was born in Argentina and came to South Africa with her husband, whom she met while studying in Europe. It was the early 1960's. Her husband and his family opposed apartheid and voted for the progressive opposition, but like many other whites, they thanked God that their party was not winning the elections, says Burton. She is not cynical by nature, but the years have taught her to delve behind the slogans and cliches; an impressive woman. She volunteered for a charity organization operating in the black townships. The more she struck roots in her new country, the more questions she had. At one stage, she found herself among the women of the "Black Sash," a sort of South African version of Women in Black. The two organizations are in contact with one another. It was legal, extra-parliamentary activity. She was arrested only once, briefly. The Black Sash women opposed the granting of amnesty to apartheid criminals.

This, in fact, caused Burton to rethink acceptance of her appointment to the 17-member commission, who were selected by President Nelson Mandela from a list of candidates who were approved after a hearing that was broadcast on television. None were politicians; most were former anti-apartheid activists. It is not the sort of appointment that is easily turned down, and Mary Burton eventually overcame her hesitancy.

It was a difficult experience. Members of the commission were divided into three sub-committees: one heard evidence of gross human rights violations from 1960 onward; a second handled amnesty requests, and a third dealt with applications for aid to victims and their families, including financial aid and compensation. The hearings went on for two years; the commission heard thousands of witnesses who were first classified by a staff of investigators. A sum of $20 million was allocated to the commission. The testimony they heard was harrowing and heartrending.

At one point or another, every member of the commission reached his or her breaking point, Mary Burton said. She reached hers when hearing a rather ordinary story: A boy of 15 had gone to a grocery store, walked into the crossfire at a demonstration, and was killed. His mother told the story to the commission, while the father sat and looked on. One commission member asked him what he would say to his son's murderers if he met them. Burton feels the question was improper, but the result was almost too hard to bear. Because the father suddenly stood up and gave vent to a colossal outburst of pain and hatred and mainly of helplessness. She does not know why this story is the one that broke her.

The commission was headed by Archbishop Desmond Tutu. He designed its hearings to be a purifying and corrective religious experience. His deputy was Dr. Alex Boraine, a Methodist minister and former MP who now teaches law in New York. Mary Burton said she and several other commission members had some serious arguments with Tutu. They objected to the religious dimension he placed on the hearings. They asked him not to appear at commission hearings wearing his priestly vestments, or to open sessions with a daily prayer. But Tutu, a Nobel Peace Prize laureate, stood his ground. It may have been more than a mere matter of style: The entire character of the proceedings, and especially the idea of amnesty being issued by the commission in exchange for revelation of the truth, was deeply rooted in Christian theology.

Often there was something superficial about the proceedings: All it took was for an individual to confess the truth and he was assured of forgiveness. It is doubtful whether this could have worked as simply between Jews and Muslims. In fact, the conflict in South Africa was resolvable because, unlike the Israeli-Palestinian conflict, it contained no elements of religious rivalry. Most residents of South Africa are Christians, and Tutu used Christianity as a source of inspiration for reconciliation.

Another source of inspiration was the doctrine of Ubuntu. There are still arguments over what had a greater effect on the character of the hearings - Christianity, Ubuntu or politics. The hearings about crimes committed by the black organizations, including those of blacks against blacks, created a "we are all guilty" atmosphere. When everyone is guilty, no one is really guilty, and some critics felt that Tutu seemed to be wrapping his purple robes around everyone. He, on the other hand, saw it as a requisite basis for national reconciliation.

Tutu often sobbed as he listened to the harrowing stories. From time to time he would also call for dramatic gestures of reconciliation. One of the heights of this came as Winnie Mandela completed her testimony. She had been called in to clarify certain facts about the death of a boy named Stompie. He was murdered at her home, evidently with her active and sadistic participation. After her testimony, Tutu suddenly called on the boy's mother to come to the front of the hall, and asked her to embrace Winnie Mandela. He had given her no prior warning. The mother, a simple woman, did as she was asked. It was a big show of reconciliation. Mary Burton was not fond of these scenes. She could barely believe her ears when I told her about my meeting with the murderers of Amy Biehl.

Amy Biehl was here

Hanging in the entry to the central Cape Town offices of the Amy Biehl Foundation is a large photograph in which she is smiling happily, a black child in her arms. Amy, her parents told me, was raised on anti-apartheid; even as a child in California she would wear hats adorned with the slogan "Free Nelson Mandela." It was therefore quite natural for her to visit South Africa as an adult. A graduate of Stanford University, she arrived as a Fulbright scholar and took on all sorts of communal projects. Working with the blacks in South Africa was a reflection of her world view: she believed in the equality of human beings and in multi-racial democracy.

Her four black killers attacked her with stones and knives - they shattered her skull and mutilated her body practically beyond recognition. Peter and Linda Biehl - rather wealthy people, not especially religious - decided to continue her life's work. The foundation they established underwrites education, health and professional training projects, as well as artistic and musical activities - all among blacks, all in the spirit of Amy.

Her four murderers were arrested, tried and sentenced to 18 years in prison. Linda Biehl was present at several court sessions. She says she felt nothing as she sat across from her daughter's murderers. Albie Sachs told her it would be good if she could make some sort of contact with the father of one of the murderers, who was also sitting in the court. At one stage, their eyes met, and at that moment she felt a sadness: He too was about to lose a son, she thought. They shook hands.

The murderers later applied to the TRC and asked that the lynch be recognized as a political act. This was the first step on the road to amnesty. They explained that before committing the murder, they had attended a gathering of a radical black group. The speaker had instructed his listeners to kill any white who happened to cross their path. Amy Biehl happened to be the one. When Peter and Linda Biehl heard of the amnesty request, they called Tutu from their home in California and said they had no objections to granting the amnesty, but that they wanted to be at the hearing. "We wanted South Africa to know that we respect the reconciliation efforts it is making, and we wanted an opportunity to tell South Africa and the entire world about our Amy," Linda Biehl explained to me.

Arriving at the hall, they found themselves in a narrow corridor. Suddenly the four murderers appeared before them. Without thinking too much, the Biehls held out their hands. Linda Biehl said that once again she felt nothing. The commission complied with the request of the four men, who were given political amnesty, and released. I asked the Biehls what they thought about the murderers. Linda Biehl says that two of them are detestable thugs, but that the other two were sort of "political soldiers." What they did was a result of the brainwashing done to them, and they had in the meantime realized their error. I asked how she knew that, at which point the conversation took a shocking turn. "Actually, they're really nice guys," says Linda Biehl. "They have nice girlfriends, and tomorrow we're taking all of them out to see the musical `Cats.'"

It was hard to hide my stupefaction. Linda Biehl said they hadn't yet seen the musical, and it was now playing in Cape Town. I asked if we were really talking about two of her daughter's murderers and their girlfriends. Had I really understood her right? She and her husband were taking them out? Linda Biehl chuckled, and said that a lot of people are amazed when they hear that she and her husband employ the two murderers in their foundation. For a second, I thought she was pulling my leg, but Linda Biehl chuckled again and said, "Oh, I thought you knew. Didn't you notice the fellow sitting by the front door next to the computer? That's Ntobeko."

While I was still wondering what to say, Mrs. Biehl called the two fellows over to join us. The other one's name is Easy Nofemela. For the next 45 minutes we sat together - Amy Biehl's two murderers, her two parents and me.

Ntobeko Penni is 28, Nofemela is 30. Both now work at the foundation. "They didn't have any work when they got out of prison, and we needed people," explained Peter Biehl. Penni actually wanted to be a social worker or journalist, but has a good head for business. The foundation is supposedly helping him to start a driving school; that is his dream. Nofemela will receive a bakery, if I understood Peter Biehl correctly. "We thought that would fulfill the reconciliation. This is the meaning of Ubuntu," says Biehl.

I told the two killers that I could not understand Amy's parents, and asked them to explain to me the meaning of the Biehl's attitude toward them. Ntobeko Penni said that he didn't really understand it, either, but that he is grateful to them. He calls Linda Biehl "Makulu" - grandmother in Zulu. Linda Biehl said it was so, and that it makes her happy, because her new grandson Alexander, born in America, also calls her Makulu. Ntobeko said that it was definitely harder for me to understand the relationship since I evidently don't understand what Ubuntu is.

Anthropologists have studied the term, and explained the concept of reconciliation that derives from it. If I correctly understand what I read, when a person harms his fellow man, he also harms the perfection of his own humanity, and thereby also harms the perfection of his victim's humanity. When the victim forgives him, he restores the humanity of the attacker, and in so doing also restores his own humanity. Ntobeko Penni explained that Ubuntu also has meaning in everyday life, the essence of which is mutual aid. If someone has nothing to eat, someone will share his bread with him. If someone has nowhere to sleep, someone will offer him a place in his home.

There are local reconciliation committees on every street, operating as quasi magistrate's courts: If two people are fighting, the neighborhood reconciliation committee sees to it that they settle the argument. Penni said that Ubuntu, which he learned in his childhood from his father, also requires one to show respect and dignity to every person, in other words, a sort of African humanism.

I said that it indeed sounded very noble and asked him, then, why he murdered Amy. "Ahhh," Ntobeko Penni said, "The rules of Ubuntu don't apply to whites." "No, no," said Linda Biehl, "Ubuntu doesn't apply to whites."

Where are the graves?

Mary Burton believes that much of the truth will come out and that this is certainly important, because, like Archbishop Tutu, she believes there can be no healing without truth. But a significant share of the truth is not being revealed. Burton is referring mainly to the acts committed by heads of the military and political establishments. Many of them just don't give a damn about the commission. The law empowered the commission to subpoena and interrogate witnesses, but too many people avoided the hearings, lied to the commission or simply ignored it. The commission missed many opportunities, says Burton. She blames herself, too. She and the other commission members weren't strong enough.

Cape Town District Court Judge Dennis Davis took me one morning on a climb to the peak of Lions Head, to gaze at the ocean. The view from the mountain top is breathtaking: Cape Town is rightly considered one of the most beautiful cities in the world. As we were climbing, the judge explained that the Truth and Reconciliation Commission's work had convinced him that the criminal law process was better suited to uncovering the truth. The fact is, Davis said, that the chance of getting an amnesty had not convinced the generals and leading statesmen to appear before the committee.

Constitutional Court Justice Albie Sachs disagrees. He unwillingly admits to the limitations of the judicial system, but in his estimate, the Truth and Reconciliation Commission exposed much more information than might have been exposed by the ordinary wheels of justice. No, Sachs confirmed, not all of the criminals had paid their debt, but the commission had achieved a great deal, including the revelation of what befell many of the people who had disappeared, and where they were buried. The commission could not bring the dead back to life, but at least it gave graves to their relatives.

Essentially, it gave them something even more important: an opportunity to tell their story. In that respect, the commission hearings are a little like the Eichmann trial. It too was designed as a sort of national spectacle, the sentence known in advance. But the trial enabled Holocaust survivors to tell a story that until then Israel was not ready to hear, and in those terms it opened up a process of national therapy. The same sort of thing happened in South Africa. That process is still in its infancy.

The Eichmann trial gave birth to an important theory about the nature of the murderer and the "banality of evil." The TRC hearings only partially confirmed this thesis. Many apartheid criminals did what they did out of a firm belief in their cause, after undergoing a gradual process of hardening. Like the Nazis, they did not see the blacks as human beings like themselves, and the more they humiliated and abused them - the more the humanity of their victims was reduced, and the ideology seemed to fulfill itself.

Transcripts of the commission's proceedings can provide an important basis for investigation of the subject, but Mary Burton says the commission did not take the required steps to ensure that its work would be properly archived and available for research purposes. She therefore fears that the hearings will in time be forgotten. But the biggest blunder has to do with compensation, she feels. That is also of greatest concern to Anglican priest Michael Lapsley.

Where is the money?

Lapsley came to South Africa from New Zealand. He did not arrive with political opinions, but influenced by the situation, he joined the ANC. Members of the group ascertained that his life was in danger, and sent him to Zimbabwe. One day he arrived home from a lecture tour in Canada and began opening the letters that had piled up in his absence. Lapsley pulled off the plastic wrapper of an envelope of theology journals, detonating the explosives inside. He remembers flying into the air; he remained fully conscious the entire time. Lapsley lost both hands and one eye. He is now the director of the Institute for Healing of Memories, a sort of workshop that helps victims of the former regime to cope with their traumas. Lapsley offers this caveat: All of the achievements of the TRC could come to naught if the government does not compensate victims of the old regime.

This was one of the commission's main recommendations. About 20,000 persons harmed by the regime are supposed to receive $2,000 each, in six annual payments, totaling approximately $40 million. But the government has not yet paid, and many victims of apartheid feel betrayed. This does not encourage a mood of reconciliation.

And it's not the only thing: South Africa is rife with unemployment, crime and Aids. About 250,000 people are dying of the plague every year; most are black. I asked Minister Kader Asmal about the compensation, and he offered, in a bureaucratic tone of voice, that the commission must first submit its final report, meaning that it must still compose two more volumes of mainly technical material. It is amazing how quickly underground fighters can adopt the excuses of government.

Kader's friends

Asmal invited me to a family celebration. He and his English wife Louise were celebrating their 40th wedding anniversary. For the occasion, they had rented the restaurant in the world-famous Kirstenbosch botanical gardens. A special menu had been printed on gold paper: The appetizers were Mediterranean style, the main course was a chicken curry. The guests, most of whom were Indian or black, came in formal wear. Many drove up in Mercedes sedans. Men with clear plastic buttons in their ears walked about in the parking lot and entrance; before being allowed into the restaurant, the guests were asked to pass through metal detectors. And Nelson Mandela was there, too.

Mandela, 85 and white-haired, radiates nobility and charisma; his presence at the celebration will presumably be considered one of the high points of the minister's career. Half a dozen ministers attended Asmal's celebration, and guests came from abroad as well, anti-apartheid activists from England and Holland. Mandela sat at the head table. Some of the guests dared to approach him; a few were honored with an invitation to sit down with him for a minute or two. He wore an African shirt, and put on an informal mood. But the whole gathering seemed like a party-event. Asmal had met his wife while working for the ANC; everything they'd been through together was related to the national struggle. Their life was the struggle, the struggle was their life.

Once Mandela left, the men loosened their ties, the minister's grandchildren recited humorous rhymes to Grandpa Kader and Grandma Louise, and there was also dancing, to music that was a blend of Western and African beats. I had the impression I was with a new elite celebrating its victory with comfort, not yet with arrogance. This, I imagined, is how Mapai insiders celebrated in the 1950s. And if they were close enough - David Ben-Gurion might even grace their parties. The future was theirs. At Tony Leon's party, on the other hand, a dejected atmosphere prevailed. The guests were bogged down in the past.

Tony's friends

Tony Leon, leader of the opposition, heads a small party that is not unlike Shinui and Meretz. It isn't easy being the opposition to a national unity government; the ANC shares rule with the National Party, which was the party of apartheid. The National Alliance, which Leon heads, recently lost a few parliament deputies, who went over to the government's side.

He is an impressive man who exudes great personal charm. Leon lives in a nice home with an Israeli wife, Michal (nee Even-Zahav), and their two children. Leon, his wife and two children attempted to set a relaxed atmosphere, but the folks gathered round the family swimming pool were in no mood to celebrate. They know, of course, that the white time is passing. I spoke at length with Colin Eglin, a MP who with Helen Suzman and others cried for many years the cry of their beloved country, of liberals and patriots against the injustice of apartheid. I believe I learned a lot from him, there alongside the Leon family pool. The zeal with which the old regime oppressed its enemies infused their struggle with hope. Eglin spoke of those days almost nostalgically.

We spoke about the rewriting of history. Everyone now uses history for political purposes. After the flag and the anthem, they start changing names of entire cities, to adapt them to the whims of the current regime. There is a new museum in Johannesburg that documents the apartheid, which visitors are required to enter in two separate lines, one for whites, one for blacks. A guide beckoned me over to a wall that commemorates over 160 underground fighters sentenced to death during apartheid. One case provides an especially detailed description. It is no coincidence that this one was selected - the judge who handed down the death sentence was Ramon Leon, whom the guide emphatically explains is the father of Tony Leon.

Regrettably, it would be hard to say that the old regime collapsed as the result of activities of the liberal opposition. Many whites now claim that they had always, either openly or not, opposed apartheid. This is also the dominant trend in the new Jewish museum in Cape Town. In a somewhat pathetic attempt to adapt history to the new situation, the museum emphasizes the part played by Jews in opposing discrimination against blacks, as if they represented the entire community. The truth is that some Jews stood out for their role in the struggle against apartheid, but most of them made their peace with the old regime and benefited from the privileges they were granted as whites. The museum does not conceal the fact, but minimizes it as much as possible.

It reminded me of a line in Nadine Gordimer's "July's People": "The present was his; he would arrange the past to suit it." Nadine Gordimer attended several sessions of the Truth and Reconciliation Commission. She can identify with those willing to forgive and reconcile, she told me, but she can also understand those who refuse to reconcile, and demand vengeance. She mostly identified with victims of the regime who refuse to forgive, but do not seek revenge. She thinks this is how she would react if she were in their shoes. She would want not want to have anything to do with the hearings, she said.


Home News
S. African court sentences Winnie Mandela to jail for fraud
By Reuters	| Apr. 25, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
PRETORIA - A South African court sentenced Winnie Madikizela-Mandela to jail for fraud on Friday, but the anti-apartheid heroine emerged defiant from court, punching a fist into the air as supporters shouted "Viva".

The flamboyant former wife of Nelson Mandela, convicted on Thursday of dozens of counts of fraud and theft in a bogus bank loan scandal, maintained her innocence and has appealed.

Saying he saw "not one hint" of remorse, Pretoria Magistrate Peet Johnson sentenced Madikizela-Mandela, 66, to five years in jail with one year suspended. However, he cited a legal provision that would let a parole board cut the term to as little as eight months.

Madikizela-Mandela immediately resigned from parliament and posts in the ruling African National Congress (ANC), signalling the likely end of a maverick political career of a woman dubbed "the Mother of the Nation" for her fight against apartheid during her former husband's 27-year imprisonment.

Madikizela-Mandela and her co-accused, broker Addy Moolman, were found guilty of stealing one million rand ($136,986) in the elaborate fraud scheme. Moolman, who also appealed, was sentenced to seven years in prison, with two years suspended.

State prosecutors said letters bearing Madikizela-Mandela's signature were used to secure loans for bogus employees of the Women's League of the ANC, which she heads.

Madikizela-Mandela's lawyers accused Moolman, bank staff and Women's League employees of lying about her role. They said she was duped into participating in a fraud she knew nothing about.

Johnson said Madikizela-Mandela was no "modern-day Robin Hood" and said she appeared to have arranged the fraud to help friends and associates. He said he recognised her role in the anti-apartheid struggle but that was not enough to get her off.

"The message has to be sent out that this type of behavior is unacceptable and will not be tolerated, no matter who the transgressor," the magistrate said.

Both defendants, both released on bail pending appeal, were also found guilty of theft charges stemming from charges they set up a phoney funeral insurance scheme and then pilfered money from participants' accounts.

Dressed in a beaded headband, white flowered kaftan and traditional bead necklace, Madikizela-Mandela showed no reaction as the sentence was passed.

Two supporters briefly interrupted court proceedings to shout support for Madikizela-Mandela, who is known as "Mama" to many of the poor township youths she has taken under her wing.

"You are the ANC, Mama!" one activist yelled in the stunned courtroom, before being hustled out.

Hundreds of people gathered outside the court, many chanting anti-apartheid slogans in support of their heroine. Ranks of police, some in riot helmets, blocked off the court entrance.

Madikizela-Mandela briefly appeared on the courthouse steps after the sentence, raising her fist in a defiant salute to cheers before bodyguards hustled her back in the building.

At a separate court hearing in Cape Town's High Court, Madikizela-Mandela's legal bid to stop parliament's ethics committee publicly reprimanding her for failing to declare income was dismissed and she was ordered to pay costs.

In a statement, Madikizela-Mandela said she would resign from parliament as well as from her ANC posts.

"The ANC is my home. Its ideals are my passion and its endeavours to make South Africa a just society is a cause that I will not relinquish. I will remain a dedicated, committed and loyal member of the ANC," she said, adding that she was sure her innocence would eventually be proved.

The move appeared to end her political career, although she has defied critics who wrote her off in the past.

Despite her widespread popularity, Madikizela-Mandela's reputation has been tarnished by a series of legal problems. She was detained and put under house arrest by the apartheid administration but has never served a jail term.

The most serious past conviction was in 1991, for kidnapping and being an accessory to assault in connection with the death of a 14-year-old township activist. Her six-year jail sentence was reduced to a fine on appeal.


Home News
Problematic policies
A comprehensive, if somewhat uneven, collection of articles offers a historical perspective of the Israeli Foreign Ministry's first 50 years
By Gabriel Sheffer	May 2, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
"Ministry for Foreign Affairs: The First Fifty Years," edited by Moshe Yegar, Yosef Govrin and Arie Oded, The Ministry for Foreign Affairs, printed by Keter Press, 1,211 pages, NIS 168

The extensive media discussion surrounding the appointment of the foreign minister in "the second Sharon government" once again underlined a certain paradox with respect to the Foreign Ministry. On the one hand, the ministry's scope of activity, the actions and moves taken within the ministry's realm of responsibility, and the various appointments there earn more coverage than the realms of responsibility and activity at other ministries (the Prime Minister's Office and the Israel Defense Forces, of course, excepted). Thus, it is difficult to compare the quantity of reports on the foreign minister and foreign policy to those published, for example, on the education and welfare ministries. However, despite this exposure, despite the ostensible prestige of the foreign service and despite the generally high quality of the personnel who serve in the ministry, its status in the government system and its influence on the determination of policy are quite marginal. And to put it mildly, the minor status and influence of the ministry, which are forced upon it by the prime minister and the Defense Ministry, are far from satisfactory, according to its senior personnel, ambassadors and various state representatives.

Now, added to the many publications, including collections of documents, memoirs by ministers, ambassadors and other envoys, and books written by academics and journalists, is this huge collection before us. This volume contains more than 1,000 pages and is comprised of no less than 129 articles and essays written by 85 Foreign Ministry employees. The management of the Foreign Ministry initiated and funded the publication of the volume to mark the 50th anniversary of Israel and the ministry, but apparently it left the decisions concerning the publication in the hands of the three editors, who in the past were ambassadors and filled senior positions at the ministry and have written a great deal about these matters.

Because of the extent of the volume, the many subjects with which the articles deal, the numerous kinds of articles and their varying quality, the editors of such a volume are not to be envied, nor is the reviewer or critic of this book, including the writer of these lines, who must evaluate such an extensive and varied "product." Therefore, this will not be a focused examination of the contents of the various articles and their quality, but rather a general evaluation of a number of basic issues that have to do with the status of the Foreign Ministry, the value of the book and its contribution to the understanding of this important subject.

`Revisionist' approach

In this spirit, first a few basic comments with respect to the status of the Israeli Foreign Ministry, which the volume is intended to reflect. As Moshe Yegar notes in his important article ("The Foreign Ministry: Structure, Work Methods and Conclusions"), in the early years following the establishment of the State of Israel, the ministry's status was not inferior relative to the Prime Minister's Office, the Defense Ministry and the IDF. Despite David Ben-Gurion's totalitarian image and the commonly held perception that he was in absolute control, ostensibly, of all policy issues - thanks to the political standing and interest of the founder of the ministry, the first foreign minister and second prime minister, Moshe Sharett, until the latter's resignation from the government of Israel in 1956 - he himself and the Foreign Ministry participated to a very great extent in setting policy in the area of foreign affairs and defense, as well as in tangential matters that touched upon many crucial issues. Therefore, in this area and other areas in which Israeli policy was established during that period, there has to be a serious historical and analytical revision by the "new historians" and a true liberation from fixed notions in Israel's short history. The volume under review here can help considerably in this revision.
