Home News
Letters to the Editor
Jun. 25, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Betting the house with us in it

Treasury policies under the leadership of Ariel Sharon and Benjamin Netanyahu are a riddle that no one can seem to solve. The economy is in deep recession; citizens struggle daily under economic decrees. It seems none of the cabinet ministers is losing any sleep over the dramatic decline in middle-class living standards, severe cuts in social allowances to those who most need them, and the addition of thousands of people to the ranks of the unemployed.

The finance minister promised us growth from the economic plan. He promised to bring back the foreign investors and promote tourism, if we only clenched our teeth a bit more. Last month, we began to understand what is going to lead us to the longed-for growth. After prolonged lobbying by Mifal Hapayis executives and by private investors - with their own interests - we were told the economic program, which struck down any remnant of social solidarity, would apparently be followed by another economic "stroke of genius." Casinos will be built.

The plan is for five casinos, which its supporters say would increase state revenues from the taxes levied on gambling, would curb illegal gambling, provide jobs for hundreds of people, and boost tourism. A casual observer can only be amazed by the magic solution and ask, "Why didn't we think of it sooner?"

The answer is, we did think of it sooner, even gave it deep thought over 50 years ago, when the state established Mifal Hapayis and after that a council for regulating gambling in sport. The young state, seeking to battle the scourge of illegal gambling, created two institutions to supervise lotteries and sports gambling. The founders of these two bodies clearly meant to channel their revenues into building up the country and developing education, social welfare and sport. The word casino did not enter their minds.

Since then, society has changed in nearly every aspect. As in other countries, individualism has put down roots and some of the value systems we were raised on have been undermined in our own backyard. Now the dog is walking the master - Mifal Hapayis, a government body earning millions of shekels a year now wants to increase profits and is negotiating with the Prime Minister's Office over a casino. The absurdity, or the arrogance, of this seems infinite.

Let us assume that the arguments of those who favor the plan are correct and state revenues will increase. Let us even assume that jobs will be created in peripheral areas, and that criminal activity related to gambling will be reduced, although the experience of the Turks was the exact opposite.

Is the sale of the State of Israel to Lady Luck the treasury's ingenious economic solution. Having damaged every area of our lives, is the treasury now to persecute our souls?

I call on economic advisors, ministry professionals, and anyone with any brains in their heads to stop this deal with the devil, and start to think of more constructive solutions to help the economy. Let us do so before we bring on new troubles - more organized crime, more prostitution, more drug trade avenues, not to mention the gambling addiction that is quietly becoming a national plague.

If this goes ahead, tens of thousands of Israeli households will be ravaged. It will increase the burden on social welfare services and cost the taxpayer hundreds of millions of shekels. The mere consideration of legalizing gambling will hamper the police and judicial system in pursuing criminals and grants legitimacy to illegal gambling.

Are we consciously prepared to let those leading the government bet the house, with us inside it? Anyone looking for easy money will pay dearly for it in the long run.

Ophir Pines-Paz, MK

Chairman, Knesset lobby against a casino

Not a democracy

Regarding "A French Christian state," by Amnon Rubinstein, June 17

The article brings up some interesting problems that, in general, immigrants face as they try to adjust to societies with beliefs different from their own. Although there is some truth to the article, it twists the Muslim view out of proportion completely.

This, however, is not my complaint, as I would expect to see this in any propaganda vehicle. I am writing because I was stunned by the last paragraph, which undoubtedly was the purpose of the article, in which Rubinstein wonders why the same people who see France as a democracy see a contradiction with the words "Jewish" and "democratic."

I believe I can clear this up. It's not a case of the words "Jewish" and "democratic," but "Israel" and "democratic." A quick perusal of some news stories from the same day this editorial appeared can easily explain this.

Take, for example, Gideon Alon's story which reported that Palestinians marrying Israeli citizens would be denied citizenship. Legislation such as this is reminiscent of South African apartheid or the Indian caste system, where one is judged based on what, not who he is.

In France, Mr. Rubenstein presents a situation where a certain group of people demand special treatment, so it is perhaps understandable how controversy could arise. But denying citizenship to the spouse of an Israeli citizen based only on the spouse's heritage is a completely different issue, and is certainly not democratic, nor fair to those citizens who wish to marry Palestinians.

Although I may know the general feelings of Muslims in France, I cannot say that I know what each one feels. In the same manner, although there are Palestinians intent on the destruction of the Israeli state, not all feel that way. So why would Israel pass such a general law?

America, my country, is far from a perfect democracy, although I would be hard-pressed to find a better one. But during the cold war, we did not prevent Russians who wed Americans from becoming citizens, even though we had been taught since infancy that the Russians were intent on our destruction.

These individuals may have been subject to scrutiny or, in extreme cases surveillance, but they were never denied citizenship. Again, not a perfect democracy, but a pretty good one.

Those who link the words "Jewish" and "Israel" will definitely see a contradiction when using the word "democratic." Israel is not a democracy, and it never will be until it treats all people of the world equally.

Steve Faucher

New York, NY

Get back to teaching

Regarding "Not making the grade," by Relly Sa'ar, June 16

The disgraceful results in math and comprehension recently reported should come as no surprise to anyone. An enormous amount has been written about the effect of budget cuts and pedagogical methods. I would suggest that the subject of school discipline is in sober fact at the root of the educational crisis.

Everyone knows that teachers today have no disciplinary power, and that they are at the mercy of rowdy and often violent pupils - and their parents. Pupils who can and wish to learn in many cases cannot because of constant disruptions in class, which the teachers are powerless to prevent.

The very concept of discipline and punishment in schools has become taboo. Teachers are no longer the object of respect. It is well known that in countries in which strict discipline is enforced, such as Russia, Japan, Singapore for example, their results are much better than ours.

The well-meaning attempts by the liberal psychology school of thought to "understand" every pupil instead of laying down the rules, has lead to a generation of children leaving school ignorant.

I would also add that schools should return to their role of teaching and leave personality development and "finding yourself" to the family.

Dr. Anthony S. Luder

Director, Department of Pediatrics Sieff Hospital, Safed


Home Print Edition Opinion
Playing with cease-fire
The cease-fire is playing with fire. In order to limit the danger that it will lead to a third intifada, worse than its predecessors, all the sides involved must stay within the bounds of the current game - stopping the violence and incitement on one side, and stopping construction of Jewish settlements and the separation fence on the other.
By Akiva Eldar	| Jun. 30, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Prime Minister Ariel Sharon has nothing to lose from the cease-fire. If all is smooth sailing it will prove that his demand to replace the Palestinian leadership was correct. If it runs aground, it will prove that Palestinian Authority Chairman Yasser Arafat is still at the helm.

If the shooting stops, personal security will improve and the economy will recover. If the shooting continues, the public will realize that there is no one to talk to and will accept more economic decrees submissively.

In either case, the expansion of Jewish settlements in the territories and more work on the separation fence will determine the borders of the Palestinian Bantustan, Sharon's revised version of apartheid South Africa's puppet states.

Palestinian Prime Minister Mahmoud Abbas, on the other hand, has quite a lot to lose from the cease-fire, either from its success or its failure. Key Fatah activists are saying they are giving him and the hated Muhammad Dahlan, his security chief, a long rope.

If the cease-fire fails, they will hang the rope from the Israeli partners and slip the noose around the necks of those who led others astray. And if the weapons are collected, the wanted men are arrested and the violence wanes, Abbas and Dahlan will be required to supply the merchandise - the end of closures and of assassinations are nothing but packaging. The real merchandise is a Palestinian state within 1967 borders, with exchanges of territories, and Jerusalem as its capital.

However, when U.S. President George Bush wondered in Aqaba how essential is Netzarim, Sharon pretended not to hear. As far as he is concerned, evacuating settlements, if ever, is an issue for 10 years time, if not more. The times are long gone by when the Israeli public, including many who defined themselves as "leftists" less than three years ago, believed that peace is the key to security.

Since the outbreak of the intifada in September, 2000, and even more so since the terror attacks of September, 2001, the assumption that security - "the fight against terror" - is the key to peace has become firmly rooted.

Thus the old equation of "territories in return for peace" has slipped out of fashion. If the occupied territories are supposed to be handed over in exchange for peace, and if peace will be achieved only after security is achieved, then at this stage there is no point in talking about territories.

Over half a year ago the members of the quartet reached the conclusion that shortcuts to a permanent status agreement could lead to a dead end. Their road map, and the brand-new security arrangements engendered by it, succeeded in taking off, for the moment, from the place were previous initiatives were grounded because it was a runway and not a destination.

In order for the Clinton formula, the Nusseibeh-Ayalon understandings or the Beilin-Abed Rabbo document to become papers of any value, a revolution of consciousness and politics will have to happen in Israel. This will be possible only after there is a separation of forces and relative calm.

The life expectancy of the hudna (tactical cease-fire) now looks longer, because for the first time a number of circles of influence have been closed.

The international circle of mainly the United States and the European Union has narrowed down the possibility of the hostile sides maneuvering between the two.

The adoption of the Saudi plan by the Arab League more than a year ago closed the regional circle and paved the way to a certain amount of Syrian pressure on the Hamas and the rejectionist organizations.

Internal Palestinian circles closed around Arafat when 13 top members of the Fatah decided the time had come to push aside the reason, or the excuse, for the humanitarian, political and economic disaster in the territories.

The appointment of Abbas as prime minister proved the major importance of the fourth circle - the government of Israel. Abbas would not have gone far without Sharon's blessing. It is possible the prime minister gambled on Arafat sabotaging Abbas and getting the road map out of his way. However, after the president of the United States embraced the new prime minister and the map, it is difficult to stop the snowball and go back to the familiar and dangerous game of throwing it at the rival's door.

The cease-fire is playing with fire. In order to limit the danger that it will lead to a third intifada, worse than its predecessors, all the sides involved must stay within the bounds of the current game - stopping the violence and incitement on one side, and stopping construction of Jewish settlements and the separation fence on the other.


Home Print Edition Opinion
Return to South Africa - 15 years down the line
My family and I left South Africa on aliyah at the end of 1987. I have not been back since, until this month, when a last-minute family event brought Marlyn and me back to S.A. for a short, hectic, wonderful visit, 15 years after leaving. It was a strange experience; everything was the same, yet nothing was.
By Larry Butchins	Jul. 7, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
My family and I left South Africa on aliyah at the end of 1987. I have not been back since, until this month, when a last-minute family event brought Marlyn and me back to S.A. for a short, hectic, wonderful visit, 15 years after leaving.

It was with mild trepidation that I boarded the El Al flight for Johannesburg. What would I find on the other end? How would I feel about visiting my old haunts, seeing old friends and family, left so long ago. How would I feel about the "new" South Africa with all its promise and problems.

In a sense, this trip was about closing circles. In Durban, it was visiting my parents' graves in the Redhill Cemetery, walking across trimmed lawns to pay homage to friends who had passed away long before their time, visiting my niece's tombstone at Stellawood - she was less than a year old when she died of typhoid in 1986. It was also about renewing acquaintances with old friends, finding that in 15 years nothing had really changed between us, and meeting new family members.

We started our 13-day whirlwind trip in Johannesburg, now almost unrecognizable since I lived and worked there 30 years ago. The international airport is magnificent; the customs and passport officials, all black, are friendly, courteous and helpful.

The place is buzzing. Thousands of international visitors, business people and tourists, arriving every day. Flights from all over the world pull up to the terminal every hour. Foreign languages are everywhere, foreign currency flows. But the rand still languishes around 8 to the dollar, 13 to the pound. Although prices in South African terms are high, when local prices are converted to dollars, you can hardly believe your luck - a succulent steak for R75 ($9); a luxurious villa with all the trimmings, garden, swimming pool, electrified fence and so on, for only R1 million (the $120, 000 that would buy a two-room apartment in Holon).

Nicely pricey

But when you're earning in rands everyday things are pricey. I was told that until recently a reasonably "good" salary on which a family could afford some of the good things in life, including live-in servants, netted out at about R14,000 a month. Today, a family needs two salaries to live at the same standard. But, and it's a huge "but" for former South Africans in Israel, you can buy a house - even in the million-rand bracket - with just a 10-percent deposit. Everybody drives leased cars - Mercedes, BMWs, a wide range of SUVs, and there are generally two vehicles in the driveway, if they haven't been stolen in the last 24 hours.

Johannesburg is still the big, raucous, brash and fast-moving city it always was, except now the city stretches from Vereeniging in the south, with Midrand joining Johannesburg to Pretoria in the north. The East and West Rand are contiguous to Johannesburg and there is hardly any space between any of these areas. It's megalopolis. Highways are plentiful, broad and fast moving, except in rush hour when they grind to a halt as in any big world city.

We didn't go into the center of Johannesburg - we had been warned - but we did manage to get a glimpse of the CBD from the M1 Highway which skirts the eastern side of the city. It looked pretty much the same, except for a huge building or two and the new Nelson Mandela Bridge which replaced the old Queen Elizabeth Bridge linking the city to Braamfontein.

Social harmony

In fact, this seemed to be our recurring impression of the new South Africa. Everywhere we went, and Durban most of all, seemed so familiar and yet so strange. It was the closest I have felt to being in a time warp, as if I had fallen asleep one night like Rip van Winkel and woken 15 years later. Everything was the same yet nothing was.

Socially the new South Africa is fantastic. The interaction between black and white, among people of the rainbow nation, is quite remarkable. From what we could see in the malls, restaurants, coffee shops, department stores - Woolworths still rules - people are relaxed, totally at ease.

But this is superficial, seeing as a foreigner, even if one had lived there in the dark days of apartheid. The country still has a long way to go. There are enormous challenges. There is unemployment, mainly among whites facing the competition of "affirmative action" in the workplace.

As one young cousin, a highly qualified stockbroker who had worked on the JSE for seven years and now cannot find a place in his profession, quipped: "If you're white, male and qualified, forget it; there are no jobs available." He and his young wife are seriously considering leaving for England.

There is still black poverty - a lot of it. We saw shanty towns on the outskirts of Johannesburg and Cape Town every bit as grim as they were in the "old days," but Alexandra and Soweto have running water and electricity.

Corruption is said to be rife and AIDS is decimating the black population, causing one of the serious problems - AIDS orphans left to their own devices, without a home, money or job prospects. They roam the streets of downtown Johannesburg, Durban and Cape Town, forming the core of a huge social problem the government is reacting to extremely slowly.

Crime is a widespread epidemic. There is scarcely one person we know who hasn't been a victim. My sister in Johannesburg had her car stolen, was mugged, lost all her jewelry, and had her house broken into, despite an arsenal of burglar alarms and a huge German shepherd. All this happened in a period of 3 months.

Defensive shields

Every individual house, gorgeous and luxurious as it may be, is surrounded by a massive wall, topped by electronic trip wires and alarms. Security company shields adorn every gate. When entering a driveway, you stop to check if anybody is approaching from any distance before opening the gate. A good friend of ours in Durban had a gun thrust in his neck as he was getting out of his car in his garage. Now he has full length mirrors on the garage wall and doesn't leave his car until the garage door is closed.

As an Israeli, there is a darker side to South Africa, which is more than disturbing, it is downright scary. It was seen in the World Anti-Racism Conference last year and is clear in the outright anti-Semitic and anti-Israeli attitudes of a large part of the population.

It is fanned by the incitement of the Mullahs and leaders of the huge Muslim communities, especially in the Cape. It is abetted by the fawning self-hatred of a small number of the South African Jewish community themselves.

There were questions about the wall being built between Israel and the Palestinian areas. Why? It's not fair, what do we hope to achieve? The poor Palestinians. This comes from people who live in designer prisons, behind high walls, with electronic anti-intrusion devices, barbed wire, vicious guard dogs, and rapid response systems, all aimed at keeping out those who want to share their wealth. The irony was lost on them.

There are other attitudes, more militant and right-wing than any West Bank settler - "I don't understand your prime minister. Why doesn't he just go in and finish the job?

The final leg of our rush through South Africa was Cape Town, and we had forgotten just what a gorgeous city it is. However, it wasn't the drive down to Cape Point, or out to the Spier Wine Estate in Stellenbosch, or even the magnificent Waterfront shopping and entertainment complex that topped our Cape Town trip. It was a short voyage across 11 kilometers of Table Bay to Robben Island that will be a lasting memory.

We had a choice, being in Cape Town only for two and a half days, of going up Table Mountain or out to Robben Island. As a returning South African for whom Robben Island was the most sinister symbol of apartheid South Africa, there was no choice. I had to close this final gap.

Served with pride

Arriving at Robben Island dockside, one is greeted with huge enlarged photographs of early prisoners arriving on the same dockside. And then you walk toward the prison enclosure itself. The gateway, bearing its decades old engraved message, "Welcome to Robben Island - Welkom in Robbeneiland," and the boastful prison service motto, "We serve with Pride - Ons Dien met Trots," is alarmingly resonant to any Jew. People were incarcerated here because they dared to think differently, to be different, to be racially different from the rulers who believed they were the pinnacle of mankind.

The tour guides on Robben Island are former black prisoners and former white guards and this more than anything shows just how far the new South Africa has come from its dismal past. They live and work side by side now, no one better or worse than the other, no one controlling or being submissive to the other.

It was strange to hear the tour guide telling a group made up largely of foreigners, the stories of the prisoners on Robben Island - Mandela, Sobukwe, Sisulu, names which I as a young journalist on a Johannesburg Sunday newspaper those long years ago, knew so well.

It was surreal to hear the tour guide talking about those whites who fought the good fight for justice in the old South Africa - Helen Suzman, Benjamin Pogrund, people I had met, interviewed, written about and frankly hero-worshipped in my days in the newsroom, covering social and political events.

And frankly, I felt a swelling of pride that I had been involved, albeit in the most minute of ways, vicariously, in the struggle which has given birth to the brave new South Africa. Our trip to the Cape ended too quickly and it was back to Johannesburg for one night.

We went to the airport early Monday morning, boarded El Al 767, and within nine hours were back on our own brave new world beyond the gates of Ben Gurion Airport.

We had been away exactly 13 days, met 86 family members and friends, spent two days viewing game in the Pilansberg, traveled across South Africa from Johannesburg to Durban to Cape Town and back, and now we were back in the sweltering mid-summer Tel Aviv heat. We had settled a lot of unfinished business. We had seen the new South Africa and we had loved every minute of it, but now - we were home.

The writer is a marketing communications consultant.

Home Print Edition Features
Government slammed as `racist' for bill to prevent Arab family unification
By Gideon Alon	| Jul. 15, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
"Since 1994, some 140,000 Arab residents of the territories have received Israeli citizenship and have settled in the country," Deputy Attorney General Meni Mazuz told the Knesset Interior and Environment Committee yesterday. Mazuz's comments came during a discussion on a government-sponsored bill to almost completely prevent residents of the territories from receiving Israeli citizenship or residency status by virtue of family unification under the Entrance into Israel Law.

During the stormy discussion, Mazuz said the government believed that the proposed law was in the country's national interests, in light of the fact that requests for family unification had increased markedly in the past decade.

Representatives of the Arab parties harshly criticized the proposed law. MK Ahmed Tibi (Hadash) termed it "a purely racist [proposal] directed against the Arab Israeli collective;" while MK Issam Makhoul (Hadash) said the bill was anti-democratic, inhumane and a violation of international conventions.

MK Wasal Taha (Balad) said that preventing individuals from choosing their spouses was reminiscent of the Nuremberg Laws; while MK Abdulmalik Dehamshe (United Arab List) commented that the law would leave the stain of apartheid on the Knesset and state.

Committee chairman MK Yuri Stern (National Union) had a hard time maintaining order during the panel's deliberations, breaking his gavel at one point in the discussion. He said he intended to push the legislative process ahead rapidly, as the government viewed the bill as vital. He said he would try to complete the process by the end of the Knesset's summer sitting on July 30.

Herzl Gadj, the director of the Interior Ministry's Population Registry, said that at present in the Galilee, there were 64,000 people who had come into Israel as Jordanian visitors to the Palestinian Authority. He added that in the period 1999-2002, the Interior Ministry had received 22,000 requests for family unification. Some 16,000 had been approved, he noted.

Home News
Mandela celebrates 85th birthday with star-studded banquet
By The Associated Press	| Jul. 19, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
JOHANNESBURG - Hollywood stars, royalty, politicians and even Nelson Mandela's cook and gardener joined him at a banquet Saturday to celebrate his 85th birthday.

Former U.S. President Bill Clinton, his wife Hillary and their daughter Chelsea were among the 1,600 guests from around the world at the highlight of a series events to honor the former South African president who celebrated his birthday Friday.

Walking up the red carpet to the sounds of a marimba band and an African choir, stars of the stage, screen and the catwalk like Robert De Niro, Naomi Campbell and Oprah Winfrey entered the Sandton Convention Center for what has been described as "the party of the Millennium."

Sporting heroes rubbed shoulders with former political prisoners, musicians and millionaires.

"It was a massive operation," said Professor Jakes Gerwel Chairman of the Nelson Mandela Foundation describing the banquet's planning.

"I'm just waiting for my bear hug," quipped his wife Febe, who said she had not yet had an opportunity to congratulate Mandela on his birthday.

South African President Thabo Mbeki and his wife Zanele arrived shortly before Mandela and his third wife Graca Machel. They celebrated their fifth wedding anniversary on Mandela's birthday.

Mandela is respected and admired across the world, to South Africans, who call him by his clan name, Madiba, he is a superstar of near-mythic proportions. He is regarded as a hero who preached racial reconciliation to the apartheid-scarred nation despite the 27 years he spent imprisoned by the white, racist regime

He won the nation's first all-race elections after the fall of apartheid in 1994, retired in 1999. But he is as popular as ever.

The banquet was also attended by former South African President F.W. de Klerk who released Mandela from prison in 1990 and who shared with him the Nobel Peace Prize for their efforts at reconciliation.

Earlier Saturday, Mandela attended the funeral of his daughter-in-law Zondi Mandela who died this week from pneumonia. She was the wife of his only remaining son Makgatho born to Mandela and his first wife, Evelyn Ntoko. Their other son, Thembi was killed in a car accident in 1969 while Mandela was in prison

Unable to attend the banquet was Raymond Mhlaba, who was jailed for life with Mandela in 1964. He was in hospital after suffering a mild stroke.

"It's a great pity for him because he really wanted to be at Madiba's side," said family spokesman Vuyo Toto. "These old men have gave come a long way together."

Also at the banquet were South Africans Callie and Monique Strydom who made world headlines when they were held hostage for more than four months in the Phillipines by the Abu Sayyaf a small Muslim extremist group.

"We are honored and privileged to be able to share such a special time with Madiba - there is only one of his kind in the world," said Monique Strydom before the party.

"I'm very excited about being with him on this milestone," said Johannesburg businesswoman Suzanne Weil.

Clinton earlier Saturday praised Mandela as a "gift to humanity" while presenting the inaugural Nelson Mandela Lecture in which he said the world should do more for Africa.

"In my lifetime only two people have made the personal journey as the leaders of their nations in the rough and tumble world of politics, Mahatma Gandhi and his worthy successor, Nelson Mandela," Clinton said to applause.

In his speech he laid out a three-step plan he said Western nations could use to help raise Africa out of poverty. It included relieving foreign debt, increasing trade and helping in wealth creation to "unleash huge amounts of resources" that could be freed for development.

He spoke at length about his family's attachment to Mandela, which began when he and his daughter Chelsea watched an early morning television broadcast of Mandela "walking to his freedom" in 1990.

"I am only his third biggest fan in my family," Clinton said, bringing a wide smile to Mandela's face, who received a hug from Hillary Clinton.

"We can't work for Africa, or against Africa, but must work with Africa," he said.


Home Print Edition Features
Wall-eyed
Now Moshav Nir Ziv wants a wall to separate it from the neighboring Arab town of Pardes Snir. `Soon they will turn us into a ghetto,' says one resident of the town
By Aryeh Dayan	Jul. 22, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
The 300-meter strip of land between the eastern edge of Moshav Nir Zvi and the western edge of Lod's Pardes Snir neighborhood not only separates two neighboring Israeli communities, but also symbolizes the border between a poor, underprivileged third world and a wealthy, developed first world. The gap between these neighboring communities will become even more tangible if and when Moshav Nir Zvi implements its plan, which has already obtained the backing of the government and Lod Municipality.

The moshav members want to build a four-meter by 1.5-kilometer wall that will totally separate their moshav from the adjacent neighborhood. They would like to build the wall around 20 meters away from the neighborhood's westernmost homes, i.e., around 300-400 meters from the moshav's easternmost homes. Pardes Snir residents will emerge from their homes and face a giant wall encircling their neighborhood from several sides; Moshav Nir Zvi residents will emerge from their beautiful homes and will no longer see the not-so-nice homes of their neighbors.

This is not the only separation wall now under construction in Israel. In addition to the separation fence going up east of the Green Line, a similar wall is also being built between the lovely villas of Caesarea and the homes of the village of Jisr al-Zarqa. Both cases involve a wealthy Jewish population seeking to build a wall to physically separate them from a neighboring and poorer Arab population.

New immigrants from Argentina established Moshav Nir Zvi in 1954 and everything built there was carefully planned. The large homes surrounded by vast lawns, the roads and paths connecting them are paved, signposted, and well maintained. Decorative streetlamps illuminate them at night and tall and stately trees provide shade during the day. The hundreds of families living on the moshav have at their disposal a swimming pool, library and a new community center.

Pardes Snir, which started on agricultural lands that the state handed over to several Arab families in 1951 in exchange for vast properties it appropriated from them in the Poleg river area, was not, in contrast, planned at all nor did it receive any kind of government funding. The result: stores and craft shops are mixed into a densely crowded residential area; the access roads are nothing more than dirt paths, exposed to the sun during the day and completely dark at night. Only around 15 years ago, when the Ganei Aviv neighborhood was being built to its east, Pardes Snir residents could connect their homes, at their own expense, to the new Jewish neighborhood's sewerage system. In Pardes Snir, where thousands of families live, there isn't a single public garden or playground. It does have, however, around 10 drug sales stations (caspomatim - ATMs - in the local lingo) that operate freely and openly.

Heading for boiling point

The tensions created in recent years between Pardes Snir and Moshav Nir Zvi may reach a dangerous boiling point if the separation wall is built. Moshav members say the need to build the wall is due to the moshav's constant exposure to unending provocations from and burglaries by Pardes Snir residents. The drug sales points draw drug dealers and addicts from all over the country to the neighborhood. On the way there, they pass through the moshav and occasionally break into some of the homes there.

"There's no law or enforcement there," complains Arye Efrat, one of the founders of Nir Zvi and currently the deputy chair of the Emek Lod regional council. "They sell drugs in caspomatim there and there is haphazard construction, which also eats away at our land. We came to Nir Zvi to find a quiet corner and day and night, they're getting on our nerves."

"The proximity to Pardes Snir," adds Colonel (res.) Amir Sheleg, the leader of the campaign to build the wall, "has become a nuisance that affects our standard of living. Gangs of drug dealers are fighting for control there. They shoot at each other and the bullets graze our homes. We are also exposed to the intense hatred of us as well as unending vandalism. We had to get rid of our orchard industry because of the thefts they perpetrated and the damages incurred."

Sheleg says he "like many others in Nir Zvi," takes a moderate political stance "and I even voted for Meretz." He says he would also be willing to agree with those Pardes Snir residents who claim that the crime and violence there are an inevitable result of the ongoing discrimination and deprivation. "But," he says, "I don't understand why I have to pay the price of this policy of discrimination."

The request to build the wall was first put forward in 1999, when the government of Benjamin Netanyahu launched a program to revive Lod. According to the plan drawn up at that time, the planning authorities were to approve the illegal construction in Pardes Snir, the municipality was to invest in building infrastructure and the government was to have finance paving a wide two-way road encircling the neighborhood. However, Moshav Nir Zvi, on whose lands a part of this road would have been paved, made its agreement to the plan contingent on construction of the wall.

The plan was frozen because the municipality refused to take upon itself funding the construction of the wall. The plan was revived around a year ago when Prime Minister Ariel Sharon sought to move ahead with reviving the city of Lod. Moshav Nir Zvi members again gave their consent to the plan contingent on construction of the wall and Sharon, who accepted their terms, found a way to deal with the issue of funding: in July 2002 he submitted a draft proposal stipulating that construction of the wall would be financed by the finance and transportation ministries to the government for approval.

The cabinet ministers realized incidentally, that they could not call a spade a spade: the decision they voted on did not refer to a separation wall, but to an "acoustic wall" that would protect Moshav Nir Zvi residents from the noise from the new road. In the detailed plan for the wall submitted by the Lod municipality to the local planning committee, the term "acoustic wall" is not mentioned.

Unreasonable to the extreme

Some two weeks ago, following the submission of the plan to the planning committee, the Pardes Snir residents' committee appealed to the High Court of Justice against the decision to build the wall. "The government's decision is unreasonable to an extreme degree and leads to an unreasonable and unfair result," wrote Attorney Hisham Shabaita, of the Human Rights program at the Tel Aviv University faculty of law, who represents the residents' committee. His central argument is that the government, under guise of building an acoustic wall, has in effect decided to finance the construction of the wall whose sole purpose is to divide population segments "against a national and class-related backdrop."

Shabaita is planning to oppose the wall in the planning committees as well. "The decision is being presented as a planning one, but in effect it's a serious political and social decision," says Nili Baruch, an urban planner active in Bamakom, an organization that defends human rights in the planning sphere and which is help attorney Shabaita in the preparation of his planning objections.

"This wall is not coming to solve acoustic problems that nobody has bothered to check even exist, but to separate between stronger and weaker populations and further compartmentalize the weaker segment." Such walls, says Baruch, were built in South African cities during the apartheid era and between Catholic and Protestant neighborhoods of Belfast. "In all cases," she adds, "the walls only increased the tension between the groups."

The plan to build the wall has already stirred emotions in Pardes Snir and prompted residents to refer to their neighbors in Nir Zvi as "snobs" and "racists." "I have no doubt that Nir Zvi is suffering to a certain degree from the situation in our neighborhood," says Araf Muharab, a neighborhood resident and Lod city council member, who is among the petitioners against the wall, "but we are suffering a lot more than they are. Instead of understanding that we are the main victims of the drug dealing that goes on here, they are making us out to be the ones responsible for it."

"The thieves who break into homes in Moshav Nir Zvi don't come from our neighborhood," says Ali Abu-Katafan, a former drug addict who lives in Pardes Snir and makes a living mending window shutters in the area. "The ones breaking into their homes are Jewish drug dealers who come here from all over the Dan region. And they only come here because the police prefer that all drug dealing be concentrated here, in the Arab neighborhoods of Lod, instead of being spread out in Rishon Letzion, Rehovot, Nes Tziona and other Jewish cities."

"The drug phenomenon here is unmatched anywhere else in the country," adds Muharab. "Every day, in broad daylight within sight of the police, 12,000 hits of drugs are sold to people who come here from all over the country in thousands of taxis. There are 50 drug dealers here, but there are no more than 50 addicts here. The drug problem in the neighborhood is not a result of internal social decline. I have no doubt that there are more youths who are drug users in Nir Zvi than there are here. Nir Zvi members portray the neighborhood as if it were the Wild West. We're not the Wild West, but a neighborhood suffering from ongoing discrimination and distress and where most of the residents are decent people who work very hard to earn a living despite the drug dealers surrounding them."

Muharab sees the move to build a wall as another expression of a broader process, which "if not halted, will transform Israel into a country of one wall after another. Those members of Nir Zvi who want to build the wall want in effect to totally separate themselves from the Arabs," he says. "They don't want to see us and they think if they build a wall they really will stop seeing us." That, in his opinion, "is a very dangerous process" because "after Nir Zvi, the residents of Ganei Aviv will ask to build a separating wall and then we'll find ourselves surrounded by walls on all sides."

This process is dangerous, he warns, "because the wall will also choke the neighborhood and turn it into a ghetto. It will create a destructive psychological effect among the residents because it will make it clear that they are unwanted, that they are in jail."

Muharab believes that not all Nir Zvi residents are in favor of building the wall. According to him, its proponents are mainly "a large group of nouveau riche Tel Avivians who joined the moshav in the last few years and for whom the wall is a means of raising the value of their property. Their motive is class-related and a matter of real estate, but the means they are using to achieve it is racist. They have to realize that the end cannot justify all the means."


Home Print Edition Features
How low can the Knesset go?
One might think that the Knesset could not go any lower, but since it began its activities in February of this year, the current Knesset has managed to break all records for shame and disgrace.
By Gideon Alon	| Jul. 30, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Last Wednesday, less than one day after Professor Asa Kasher and retired Judge Professor Yitzhak Zamir leveled very harsh criticism at the behavior of Knesset members, reminding them during a study day on ethics that they serve as public role models, Minister Meir Sheetrit fell upon the chair of the Labor Knesset faction MK Dalia Itzik from the speaker's podium, saying, "I will shut you up! What do you think? I will shut your big mouth up!"

Former minister Itzik rejoined with, "You shut your mouth, idiot! The person who can shut my mouth has not yet been born and you will not be the first."

A short while later, MK Nissim Ze'ev of Shas upbraided Interior Minister Avraham Poraz in the Knesset plenum, saying that Poraz's assistant was a "homosexual who disgusted anyone that came near him," and that therefore, according to halakha [Jewish law], he was "worse than an animal."

A few weeks earlier, MK Roni Bar-On (Likud) told his fellow faction member Gila Gamliel, "If I had a daughter like you, I would kill my wife."

These three strident chords are largely typical of the 16th Knesset. One might think that the Knesset could not go any lower, but since it began its activities in February of this year, the current Knesset has managed to break all records for shame and disgrace.

For some time now, the police's national fraud squad has been questioning four MKs under warning for falsifying a vote during the passage of the new economic plan. The four - Michael Gorlovsky, Yaakov Edri and Yehiel Hazan of the Likud and Wasal Taha of Balad - denied the accusations (with the exception of Gorlovsky, who admitted voting twice and was subsequently penalized by the Knesset Ethics Committee). Minister Avraham Poraz (Shinui) was also interrogated on this matter, but all agree he had voted twice unintentionally.

The double voting affair, which Attorney General Elyakim Rubinstein called "a serious phenomenon touching on the very foundations of democracy," is also without precedent. There have already been cases of ministers and Knesset members being convicted of crimes and even sentenced to jail sentences, but never has an attempt to influence the outcome of a vote in the Knesset plenum by falsifying a vote come to light before. Never have Knesset members been suspected of fraud, forgery and breach of trust committed within the walls of the legislature itself.

No immunity reform

Also troubling was the decision by the Knesset House Committee in May to reject a request by the attorney general to lift the immunity of MK Naomi Blumental (Likud) so that she could be indicted for corruption and election bribery. The committee decided by one vote (eight to seven) not to remove her immunity for her role in an affair that involved her paying for the accommodations of Likud Central Committee members at the City Tower Hotel in Tel Aviv. Knesset representatives, including MK Gorlovsky, together with Shas, United Torah Judaism and the National Union said no to Rubinstein.

Since then, considerable effort has been made to carry out a reform in the procedure involved in the lifting of immunity. Knesset Speaker Reuven Rivlin blew the dust off the Nissim Committee Report - a public committee headed by former justice minister Moshe Nissim - that in 1997 recommended transferring the authority for the decision on whether or not to remove a Knesset member's immunity to an external body, and convened a debate in his chambers together with the members of that committee (Moshe Nissim, Professor Claude Klein, Professor Eliezer Lederman and attorney Uriel Linn) and Dr. Susie Navot, who is writing a doctorate on the subject of immunity for Knesset members. Afterward, the Knesset House Committee held three meetings on the subject of immunity, and it was decided to oppose transferring the authority on the matter of revoking immunity to an external body. The main argument of those opposing the proposal was that such a far-reaching step would further erode the status of the Knesset and undermine its authority. Last week, coalition chair MK Gideon Sa'ar, who supports the implementation of the recommendations, once again tried to pass a bill in this reform spirit in the Knesset plenum, but was unsuccessful.

Even less far-reaching bills promoted by MKs Michael Eitan and Roni Bar-On on the subject of immunity have been removed from the agenda. Eitan proposed that the attorney general's request to lift immunity be automatically accepted, unless the suspected Knesset member asks the Knesset House Committee to debate non-revocation. Bar-On proposed that a decision by the House Committee should serve only as a recommendation to the plenum, which would ultimately decide on the matter in an open vote.

In the current term, the dispute between the Knesset and the Supreme Court over the authority of the court to annul laws passed by the Knesset that run counter to Basic Laws heated up. The tension between the two branches of government peaked in March, when new Knesset Speaker Reuven Rivlin let loose a harsh attack on the Supreme Court and the "constitutional revolution" led by the President of the Supreme Court, Justice Aharon Barak. Rivlin inveighed against the fact that a Tel Aviv Magistrate's Court judge saw fit to annul a law passed by the Knesset with the claim that it ran counter to a Basic Law. Rivlin argued that judicial activism would "destroy the trust of the public in the Knesset, a trust without which it has no raison d'etre."

Rivlin's critique did not go unanswered. Barak responded in no less a critical vein: "Do not undermine the legitimacy of the court. Criticize its decisions." In his view, Rivlin had undermined the court's very legitimacy, "something unheard of." In the wake of the exchange of blows between the heads of the legislature and the judiciary, a "cease-fire" came into effect during which the Knesset would pass a law that would determine that only the Supreme Court would be entitled to decide by a special majority on the annulment of a law, at which time the Knesset would be given the opportunity to amend the law.

In the area of legislation, the current Knesset has not yet found the time to complete the passage of important laws because only six months have passed since it began its activities. The most prominent law passed thus far is the governmental law on the battle against organized crime, which for the first time determines what organized crime is, makes it possible to impose severe penalties for those heading crime organizations and gives law enforcement authorities the tools to fight against these organizations.

There has not been any real advance in the legislation of Basic Laws. However, in the discussion held in the chambers of the Knesset speaker last month, MK Avraham Ravitz (UTJ) stunned the others present when he withdrew his opposition to the legislation of Basic Law: Legislation, which would determine the normative superiority of Basic Laws over other laws.

At the same time, the chairman of the Knesset Legislative Committee, MK Michael Eitan (Likud), advanced a long list of thorough and in-depth discussion on the subject of a "broad-consensus constitution," but the discussions, some of which were academic, are still far from ripening into legislation. As of now, the Knesset plenum knocked down four Basic Laws: Social Rights bills on preliminary reading.

Crumbling opposition

The broad coalition, which enjoys the support of 68 Knesset members, has yet to encounter a close-knit, fighting opposition. Coalition chair MK Gideon Sa'ar has been steering the coalition effectively, sometimes using an iron fist. When four Likud Knesset members, David Levy, Gila Gamliel, Haim Katz and Marina Solodkin, did not vote in favor of the economic plan, they were given disciplinary penalties. In the coalition, it is still difficult to point to Knesset members whose parliamentary activities are exemplary, with the exception of old-timers Michael Eitan and Avraham Hirschson (as chair of the Knesset Finance Committee). Among the new Knesset members, the most noticeable are Gila Gamliel, as chair of the Committee for the Advancement of the Status of Women, Roni Bar-On, as chair of the Knesset House Committee, former Shin Bet officer Ehud Yatom and the young Gilad Arden. Other promising new MKs appear to be Shinui's Reshef Chayne, Melli Polishuk-Bloch, Ilan Leibowitz and Ehud Ratzabi, National Union's Professor Aryeh Eldad and the NRP's Gila Finkelstein.

By the way, the only Knesset member that has not made a single speech in the Knesset plenum, proposed a single bill or asked a single question is the prime minister's son, MK Omri Sharon. When asked why he has not mounted the Knesset podium in the more than six months since elected, he responded, "When there is an important subject on which I have something to say, I will get up to speak. Meanwhile, I am studying the subjects, participating in the debates in both the plenum and the committees."

In the opposition, Labor faction chair MK Dalia Itzik tried to create a tight bloc that would present a united front in its struggle against the government, but in light of the diametrically opposed views on political and security matters of Shas and the UTJ on the one hand, and Meretz and the Arab parties on the other, it is difficult to conduct a common struggle on anything except social-welfare issues. The no-confidence votes against the government presented almost weekly have become an absurd tool that no one takes seriously.

It is difficult to point to any prominent Knesset members in the opposition, with the exception of a few old timers, such as Dalia Itzik and Avraham Burg (Labor), Zahava Gal-On and Ran Cohen (Meretz), Yitzhak Cohen (Shas), David Tal (One Nation) and Ahmed Tibi (Hadas-Taal). Among the opposition Knesset members of note (so far) are Yitzhak Herzog, Yuli Tamir, Danny Yatom and Orit Noked (Labor) and Yisrael Eichler (UTJ).

The hostility and tension between the Jewish and Arab Knesset members continues to characterize the debates of the current Knesset. The stormy debate over the killing of the Hamas chief in Nablus, Salah Shehadeh, or the comments by Minister Avigdor Leiberman that he is willing to provide the security prisoners with a bus that would take them to the sea are two example of the subject that have kicked up a storm in the Knesset plenum.

In one of the debates about Leiberman's buses, held in June, after Deputy Knesset Speaker Moshe Kahlon removed several Arab Knesset members from the plenum, MK Azmi Beshara (Balad) said, "The Israeli parliament must decide how it wants to be run. It is not doing anyone a favor when it listens to me. I will not regret it very much or be sorry if the Israeli parliament were to decide that it no longer wants Arabs here. Then the whole world would see that that Israel does not want Arabs, and that it is an apartheid state."


Home Print Edition Features
PA: `Our successes have turned out to be failures'
By Danny Rubinstein	| Jul. 31, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
"The State of Israel is not serious about implementing the road map," Palestinian Minister of Security Mohammed Dahlan said yesterday in an interview with the Al-Araby TV station, expressing of a growing sense of disappointment in the Palestinian leadership with the outcome of the visit to Washington by Prime Minister Ariel Sharon.

Similar statements were made yesterday by Palestinian cabinet members Nabil Sha'ath and Nabil Amr. The editorial in the Palestinian weekly, The Jerusalem Times, published tomorrow, says Palestinian Legislative Council members are expressing doubt whether Palestinian Prime Minister Mahmoud Abbas' own visit to Washington was successful, and it is possible that his government will not survive a vote of confidence by the PLC. Such a vote won't come sooner than next week.

Abbas meanwhile wrapped up his foreign travels with stops in Morocco and France. Ahmed Qureia, speaker of the PLC, who accompanied Abbas on the trip, is undergoing medical tests in Paris. When he returns to the territories he will prepare for a PLC session.

Palestinian spokesmen have been warning that what had appeared to be successes have turned into failures, and what they are losing are genuine assets. Their demand for a full freeze on the settlements, as stated in the road map, has been shoved aside, and they received the dismantling of a handful of outposts, and the actual number of outposts in the West Bank now is greater than it was at the time of the Aqaba summit, a few weeks ago.

On the prisoner release issue, President George W. Bush has accepted Israel's position that prisoners with "blood on their hands" are not going to be released, and a quarter of those who will be released are petty criminals and Palestinians caught without permits working in Israel.

Two hundred such Palestinians were caught last week in Shuafat.

The checkpoint issue led Al Quds, the West Bank's most widely distributed newspaper, to run a front page headline on Tuesday saying "IDF lifts 3 checkpoints - only 157 to go," which is why Dahlan last night raised the demand in his meeting with Defense Minister Shaul Mofaz that IDF withdrawals from Palestinian towns and cities be accompanied by the dismantling of checkpoints around those towns - otherwise the withdrawals are meaningless.

One of Abbas' confidants laughed yesterday over the headline in the Israeli press that the IDF would be withdrawing from Jericho. Every Palestinian knows Jericho is the only city in the West Bank where the IDF is not located. It has full Palestinian security control, and it has prisoners such as the secretary general of the Popular Front, Ahmed Sa'adat, a wanted man in Israel and in prison, on the basis of an agreement, in the Palestinian prison in Jericho.

The Palestinians believe the tens of millions of dollars they've received so far from Washington are very little compared to what Israel gets - and they have to pay a high price, impossibly high as far as they are concerned, of dismantling the terrorist infrastructure of Hamas and Islamic Jihad. "The hudna could collapse because Israel is violating every agreement," said Hamas and Jihad spokesmen yesterday.

There was particularly vehement criticism of what they regard as Bush's surrender on the separation fence issue. The "Berlin Wall," as Yasser Arafat called it, or "the apartheid separation fence," according to Al-Hayyat Al-Jedida, - "shows that Israel is maintaining the state of war with us," said the newspaper.

And the main point is that according to the data Abbas gave the president, the fence will involve expropriation of 13 percent of the West Bank. Losing tens of thousands of dunam of land is so far, for the Palestinians, the only tangible result of a political process that hasn't even really begun yet.


Home Print Edition Features
Nonviolence. Why didn't we think of that before?
Voices in the territories are calling for a switch to nonviolent struggle. In the Palestinian press, writers speak of the power of the weak.
By Danny Rubinstein	| Aug. 3, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
The most famous statement made by Palestinian Prime Minister Mahmoud Abbas (Abu Mazen) before he was appointed to this position was that the Palestinians need to end the "military intifada."

He wrote and spoke about this often, explaining that Palestinian groups that use guns, explosives and suicide attacks do huge political damage to the Palestinian cause. "We must conduct the struggle and the intifada without violence," he said.

The idea is not new. Years ago, there were Palestinians who recommended adopting the nonviolence used by Mahatma Gandhi against British rule in India and by Martin Luther King, Jr. in his fight for civil rights for blacks in the U.S. The most prominent Palestinian proponent of this idea was a young man named Mubarak Awad, who had been educated in America. About 10 years ago, Awad tried to interest the Palestinian public in the West Bank and Gaza with this idea. But the Israeli Interior Ministry deported him and he returned to the U.S.

Today as well, more than a few Palestinians are caught up in promoting nonviolent struggle. In general, they are part of a not particularly large circle of academics who understand the power inherent in this idea.

The Palestinian population of the West Bank and Gaza constitutes more than one-third of the inhabitants of Greater Israel. Together with Israeli Arabs and Palestinian residents of East Jerusalem, they constitute about 45 percent of this population. Sari Nusseibeh noted a few years ago, when he was a lecturer at Bir Zeit University, that if the Palestinians were to sit quietly and let Israel establish more and more settlements and annex the West Bank and Gaza, their victory would be assured.

The argument is well-known: Israel, if it attempted to swallow the West Bank and Gaza, with their three-and-a-half million inhabitants, would be suffocated. If Israel kept the Palestinians under an apartheid regime or in artificial enclaves, and if they waged their struggle nonviolently, they would quickly win the sympathies of the entire world. Israel would soon be as isolated and universally boycotted as South Africa was in its day, and it would be forced to give in.

Against this background, an organization called the Common Ground News Service (which is part of a larger organization that deals with conflict resolution) has embarked on a media campaign aimed at explaining to the Palestinian public why it is worth their while to use nonviolent methods and how such methods should be used. The campaign includes articles in Palestinian and Arab newspapers, such as Al-Hayat in London and Al-Quds and The Jerusalem Times in Jerusalem.

Thus, for example, Tufik Abu-Bachar, a member of the Palestinian National Council who lives in Jordan, wrote that in 1974, when the Palestine Liberation Organization decided to set up a state in any part of Palestine that it succeeded in liberating, that was essentially a declaration that the Palestinians do not have to wage an armed struggle, as called for in the PLO charter; rather, they can and should use diplomatic methods. He said the first intifada succeeded because it was essentially nonviolent. It is true that people threw stones, which is a form of violence, but it is a very limited one, with largely symbolic significance, calling to mind the ancient ritual that was part of the pilgrimage to Mecca (the hajj), in which the pilgrims stoned the devil with actual stones.

Abu-Bachar said the use of weapons and violence during the current intifada and the killing of civilians have caused the Palestinians to lose international sympathy. Additionally, Israelis who used to be sympathetic to the Palestinian cause have ceased to be so. He cited petitions and opinion polls that show that most Palestinians in the West Bank and Gaza believe in nonviolent struggle and think that such a struggle brings out the weakness of the stronger party, who is unable to employ his full strength against the weaker side.

Embarrassment works

Another interesting article, written by journalist Mohammed Dararma of Nablus, dealt with the use of the arts as nonviolent weapons. He described a demonstration by Palestinian musicians, actors and artists in April 2001, in which they marched on an Israel Defense Forces roadblock between Ramallah and Jerusalem singing, playing instruments and dancing. The demonstrators caused the soldiers great embarrassment. He also described demonstrations by doctors, by farmers who came to work their fields in areas to which the IDF had forbidden entry, and a large demonstration in Ramallah by activists who banged cooking pots and garbage can lids. All of these aroused enormous international sympathy.

Many people charge that Palestinian art - particularly drawings, photographs and poetry - is imbued with incitement to violence. But Dararma thinks this is part of the struggle against the occupation, and he believes that in general, Palestinian art urges life, not death.

Attorney Jonathan Kuttab described the internal debate that has raged in Palestinian society for years over whether to apply for redress to the Israeli courts. A well-known Palestinian adage warns: "If the judge is your enemy, to whom can you complain?" Kuttab's article argues that the Israeli legal system has indeed caused the Palestinians tremendous frustration, as it is a system that has granted legitimacy to land expropriations, the settlements and house demolitions. He goes into detail on the struggle against the use of torture in interrogations of Palestinian suspects and describes the Landau Committee Report, which permitted the Shin Bet to use "moderate physical pressure" during interrogations. But despite all this, he thinks that belief in the justice of the Palestinian struggle creates an opening for increased use of the Israeli legal system as one element of a nonviolent struggle.

Other people who are encouraging the use of nonviolence are the groups of volunteers from abroad who come to help the Palestinians. Dr. William Thompson, an American clinical psychiatrist, described how these volunteers manage to embarrass the Israeli government when it forbids their entry into Israel and deports those who succeed in reaching the West Bank or Gaza.

The most common methods of nonviolent struggle are demonstrations, marches, sit-ins, conferences and petitions. The Palestinians make widespread use of all of these - for instance, in the broad public campaign now being waged for the release of Palestinian prisoners. Photographs of Palestinian women carrying pictures of their jailed sons are published every day in the Palestinian press. The problem is that even quiet demonstrations have sometimes quickly degenerated into violence. Teenagers and young men throw stones, and Israeli soldiers respond with tear gas and rubber-coated steel bullets that have more than once caused serious injuries or even death.

Against the authors of this series of articles (only some of which have been published so far) and their supporters are ranged the perpetrators of the attacks - Hamas, Islamic Jihad, Fatah cells (the Al-Aqsa Martyrs Brigades) and the leftist organizations (PFLP and DFLP) - who utterly reject the idea of nonviolence. To date, it seems they have garnered more support among the public in the territories than have proponents of nonviolence. Now, however, it may very well be possible to speak of growing support for Abu Mazen's statements against "the militarization of the intifada."


Home Print Edition Opinion
The definition fence
The fence is ruthless, there's no doubt about it. However, its ruthlessness is the kind of cruelty that no great historical process can do without.
By Ari Shavit	| Aug. 7, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
True, there's something repulsive about this fence. It cuts through the country, slicing up the landscape and blocking off entire populations. It limits freedom of movement and denies right of access and prevents the movement of goods. Yes, Meron Benvenisti is right when he says the separation fence has something antigeographic, antihistorical and antihuman about it. After all, anyone who travels the length of the fence cannot escape the feeling that it is some monstrous engineering giant imposed on the hills by a super powerful entity. Anyone traveling its length cannot escape the association with East Germany.

The name is also terrible. The concept of a separation fence is indeed an apartheid concept. When one side, the stronger side, unilaterally separates itself from the other, weaker side, the connotations are indeed South African. When an armed, organized state stretches a barbed wire fence against pregnant women and hardworking people, it turns the stomach. The concrete wall that Israel is trying to place between it and the Palestinian misery is, of necessity, perceived as the brutal iron wall of mastery.

Nonetheless, the fence is vital. But, not for security reasons, nor for immediate political reasons. The fence is vital because it is not a separation fence. It is a definition fence, a fence that for the first time defines the extent of Jewish sovereignty in the Land of Israel. A fence that once and for all defines Israeli sovereignty.

Official Israel, of course, denies this. Official Israel wants to regard the fence as a security-engineering project of limited significance. However, precisely because of its oversized monstrous dimensions, it is clear the fence is a constitutional enterprise. Precisely because of its brutality in determining the facts on the ground, it is clear that it is impossible to retreat from it. The hard lines it draws in the landscape are going to be our borders. The new reality it is shaping will become the reality of our life in the coming generation.

That's no simple matter. For years, Zionism has been accused of being in constant ferment, internal and external chaos, refusing to define its limits, to say what belongs to it and what does not. The inability of the Jewish state to create for itself a defined geographic envelope doomed it to a state of perpetual disquiet in its identity. It was a situation in which it wasn't clear to citizens and neighbors, what Israelis were killing and dying for.

Now, thanks to the 600 violent kilometers of the definition fence, that matter has been decided. Israel knows its limits. For the route of the fence indeed defines Israel as a democratic Jewish nation state that covers some 80 percent of the western land of Israel. It defines it as a rational coastal country, pragmatic and life-seeking.

It is important to be precise in this. The route of the fence is not the route of peace. When there is peace, the border will more or less match the 1967 lines. However, for peace to be constituted here the Palestinians have to recognize the right of Israel to exist as a Jewish state. They have to give up the right of return. Therefore, the existential role of the fence is to create the geographic platform from which it is possible to narrow the occupation as much as possible before peace arrives. Its function is to start the lengthy, complicated surgery of defining the Siamese Israeli and Palestinian twins and separating them.

The fence is ruthless, there's no doubt about it. However, its ruthlessness is the kind of cruelty that no great historical process can do without. When the time comes, when on both sides of the new definition fence there will be clear and distinct dimensions of identity, maybe it will be possible to give up the fence, but even then it would be best not to smash it. It would be best to plant the flora of the land along it. Figs and dates, perhaps.


Home Print Edition Opinion
Letters to the Editor
Aug. 11, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Last month, the eminent Palestinian pollster, Dr. Khalil Shikaki, released a poll on the Palestinian "right of return." He arrived at a striking conclusion: "It is now clear that an Israeli recognition of the refugees' right of return does not carry with it the kind of risks that Israelis have always feared." This claim has since garnered widespread international media attention, from the New York Times to the Wall Street Journal. But sadly, Shikaki's own data does not support it.

In the poll, Shikaki presented a sample of Palestinian respondents from the disputed territories, Lebanon, and Jordan with a hypothetical situation. The respondents were told that the Palestinian leadership had been offered by Israel a proposed solution to the refugee problem along the lines of the January 2000 Taba negotiations. The polling question stipulated that only "a small number of refugees" would be allowed to "return" to Israel and that the Palestinian leadership had already accepted the terms of the proposal.

When informed of these facts, only 10 percent of the Palestinian respondents said that they wanted to "return to Israel and become an Israeli citizen."

In contrast, the strong majority of Palestinians (71 percent) preferred to reside in a future Palestinian state in Gaza, the West Bank, or their host country or to relocate to "designated areas inside Israel that would be swapped later on with Palestinian areas as part of a territorial exchange." Those who opted against relocating to Israel proper were also promised "fair [economic] compensation."

Shikaki interpreted this data to mean that Palestinians are uninterested in overrunning Israel demographically with unfettered emigration. Yet Shikaki fails to consider other interpretations, namely that Palestinians generally do not want to become "Israeli citizens" or move to Israel if only a "small number" of Palestinians will be living there. The relatively shallow support for "becoming an Israeli citizen" and "returning" to Israel in "small numbers" may indicate nothing more than a broad-based desire to relocate to Israel under more propitious circumstances. Indeed, Shikaki downplays a noteworthy finding: According to his own data, 95 percent of Palestinian respondents agree with the statement that the "right of return" is a "sacred right that can never be given up."

Furthermore, although a surprisingly large percentage of respondents were willing to resettle outside of Israel proper if the Palestinian leadership went along with it, Shikaki minimizes the true feelings of the Palestinian public. In fact, "When asked how the respondents themselves feel about the proposal," half said that they are presently opposed to compromising with Israel on the refugee problem.

Shikaki's polling data may highlight an unforeseen discrepancy between what Palestinians might accept from their leadership and their own private political preferences. But Shikaki shies away from the obvious conclusion: Palestinians might moderate their positions toward Israel if their leaders demonstrated more leadership on the refugee problem.

Finally, Shikaki's poll is deceptive because it deceives the respondents themselves. True, an unexpectedly large percentage of respondents were willing to accept "fair compensation" in return for relinquishing their dream of emigrating to Israel. But the respondents were apparently unaware that their expected compensation was wildly unrealistic. According to Shikaki's own data, 65 percent of the respondents believed that "a fair compensation should be between $100,000 and $500,000" for each refugee family. In the poll (which relatively few people have evidently probed), Shikaki himself admits, "The estimates for a fair compensation were much higher than the estimates of what would actually be paid."

Shikaki thus presented the respondents with a false choice and has since minimized the admittedly skewed results.

Regrettably, Dr. Shikaki's misleading interpretation of his data has already altered the discourse on the Israeli-Palestinian peace process. A Wall Street Journal lead editorial recently stated, "The prospects for peace are certainly brighter today than they have been at any time in the past three years....The silent majority of Palestinians seem ready for compromise, as demonstrated again recently by a poll showing that most refugees are not interested in exercising a right of return to Israel." There may well be some signs that the road map is gaining traction, but Shikaki's poll, in fact, says little to suggest that the Palestinian public has significantly moderated its position on the "right of return."

Max Abrams

Washington DC

Regarding "The Definition Fence" by Ari Shavit, August 7:

The op/ed piece jumps too quickly to assert that the fence/wall now defines the border of Israel even though Ari Shavit, admits it is "an apartheid concept." First, it overlooks the cease-fire line available since 1967 as an internationally recognized border, to which Israel agreed in signing United Nations Security Council Resolution 242. Second, it violates the road map (agreed to by Israel), which requires that the border be negotiated. Third, the negotiations were expected by the Quartet to begin with the line of Resolution 242. Fourth, Israel already has the lion's share, 78 percent, of Mandate Palestine. Does it really need the added land captured by the fence?

The fence/wall is not immutable any more that the infamous Berlin Wall. It could readily be toppled by Israeli bulldozers. I strongly believe in a secure Israel, but am convinced it won't be possible if its closest neighbor is deprived of more than the 22 percent (full West Bank and Gaza) it has agreed to.

Malcolm Fleming

Prof. Emeritus, Indiana University, Bloomington, IN

Much has been said recently about rough treatment of foreign correspondents by Israeli officials and security people. Without commenting on the various stories and anecdotes of my colleagues, here in another one.

I have been a correspondent in Israeli for the Swiss daily paper Neue Z?rcher Zeitung for the past 16 years. Recently I have been traveling in and out of Israel about once a month - with my laptop computer - without experiencing any problem.

Last month an unfortunate incident happened to me in Zurich. On my way to the airport, my hand luggage was stolen. In it were my return ticket to Israel and my passport. I convinced the border police in Zurich to let me travel without my passport. I would take upon myself the risk of being refused entry. (The carrier, Swiss, trying to earn a windfall profit, did make me buy another ticket, however.)

Upon arrival at Ben-Gurion airport, shortly before 4 A.M., I went to the booth without a line (for crews and diplomats) and explained the problem to the border policewoman: "I am a Swiss national and have arrived without my passport because it was stolen in Zurich." Fortunately I had in my possession the press card issued by the Government Press Office, which carries my picture, and presented that.

The woman called a colleague, who asked me to sit and wait. In almost any other country, democratic or totalitarian, this would have been the start of a frustrating experience with an uncertain outcome. At a minimum the wait would have lasted until 9 A.M., when the Swiss Embassy would open its office, followed by endless interrogations and verifications. To my surprise the procedure at Ben-Gurion Airport was completely different. The police woman took my GPO card to the clerk of the Interior Ministry whose office is located just behind the booths. He compared the picture on the GPO card with the picture that was in the computer, took a look at me, and told the policewoman that everything was okay. Thereupon, she issued me a visa on a piece of paper, and instructed me to get my passport stamped by the Interior Ministry in Jerusalem as soon as I got the replacement from the Swiss Embassy. The whole procedure took less than half an hour. At 4:30 A.M. I was in a taxi on my way to Jerusalem.

George Szpiro

Neue Z?rcher Zeitung


Home Print Edition Opinion
Arik `73 surrenders to Ariel `03
Recent publications on the Yom Kippur War recalled "Arik" the general, who urged his troops to "screw" the Egyptians and cross the Suez Canal. It's difficult to believe. Sharon of 2003 is passive. He rarely takes the initiative, preferring to do nothing while waiting for time to act in his favor.
By Aluf Benn	| Aug. 14, 2003 | 12:00 AM
		 submit to reddit	
Recent publications on the Yom Kippur War recalled "Arik" the general, who urged his troops to "screw" the Egyptians and cross the Suez Canal. It's difficult to believe. The behavior of the current prime minister is not reminiscent of the combative corps commander of 1973. Sharon of 2003 is passive. He rarely takes the initiative, preferring to do nothing while waiting for time to act in his favor.

Sharon's patience gave Israel political stability and public calm, which was vital under the fire of the intifada and especially after the years of quick-draw zigzagging by Benjamin Netanyahu and Ehud Barak. However after two and a half years in office, there are growing signs of the disadvantages of a leadership that only reacts and is deterred by risks.

The separation fence affair is the best example of Sharon"s weakness. For good or bad, the fence is the outstanding national project of his administration. The barbed wire and concrete walls are his Dimona reactor, his national water carrier, and his draining of the Hula swamps. True, those projects were also approved in a rather round-about manner, without sufficient public criticism, and were justified in terms of Zionist ideology and national needs.

But the history of the fence has broken the Israeli record for meandering decision making. It imposed itself on the previous government, which didn't want it, through a combination of public anxiety about terrorism and effective lobbying by a posse of politicians like Dan Meridor, Haim Ramon, and the mayors of towns on the seam line, and of senior officials, like Avi Dichter, Amos Yaron, Shlomo Aharonishki, and Uzi Dayan.

Sharon was never enthusiastic about the fence and doubted its efficacy. He vehemently opposed Barak's proposal, to unilaterally withdraw to a border according to "the Camp David map," which would have annexed the large settlement blocs. From the minute he was dragged into the project, Sharon tried to pretend it didn't exist or at least minimized its importance. Over and over he emphasized that it is neither a political nor a security fence, but a tactical obstacle to delay terrorists. That approach prevented him from turning the fence into a political card to play against the Americans.

Sharon accurately reckoned the Americans would oppose a fence in the middle of the West Bank stretching around Kedumim and Ariel. So, back in April he froze the construction in those areas.
